Yeah this sounds really angry, and I am. i'm sorry, but I am.

\n
\n
\n
\n\nOgremarco \n\n\n
\n
\n\nNovember 2007 \n edited November 2007 in Story Games
\n
\n
\n
\n
\n A little backstory. I had an argument last night and it left me a little shaken. So, yeah, I'm pretty angry about it and even more angry that i let my temper get the best of me and couldn't make a rational argument. Actually i'm even more angry that I then let my need to not be seen as a dick make me back down on something I really believe in. Does that sound wierd?
\n
\nAnyway. here goes a little rant.
\n
\nSo, if I'm not willing to come up with a business plan and sink my savings into a publishing company i might as well not bother? I have to risk my livlihood to be taken seriously? I'm a bad person who's diluting the market with crap if i'm not going to suck some industry guy's dick for a writing gig? Wanting to write a game as a hobby and just break even and be pleasantly surprised when I get some beer and con money is a spurious way to go about it? I'm not serious unless I take a huge financial risk to start a company and hope I get everything right and hope no one i work with drops the ball so I don't go bankrupt? or else i'm not serious if I don't bust my ass to sell myself to someone with money so i can either write their game or write my game with them looking over my shoulder?
\n
\nAnd small games are diluting the market?
\n
\nHow exactly does a self produced game dilute the market? how does the tiny percent of a percent that my game might make really threaten the big guys? Especally when the people buying probably wouldn't buy their product anyway because they're fed up with it?
\n
\nSuffice to say my buttons were pressed. Sorry to dump this on you, guys.
\n
\n

    \n Either gaming is a market or it isn't a market. If its a market, games are worth what people will pay for them. Prices are established by buyers and sellers coming to a negotiated price. If some blowhard can't get what they want for their game because hobbyists are out their selling at a loss, I understand their disappointment, but their business model is flawed. They want to apply consumer culture to the hobby game market, but they don't have enough product differentiation to charge the premium they want.

    As an aside, does community theater dilute the market for Broadway musicals?

    \n Clint, there's one vital piece of information I feel like I'm missing. Does this person's opinion matter to you?

    Because if it doesn't, then fuck him in the ear and don't worry about his desperate defense mechanisms. He's clinging to a structure that's being shaken by change and adaptation. It's a bad place to be, but it's not your fault that people are interested in exploring something new and different (those people being both you and potential consumers).

    But, if the person's opinion matters, especially if they're a friend, then that's rough to hear. I'm sorry that you've been put in a place where you need to choose between your personal strengths and your interpersonal allegiances. Being told by a friend that you're sabotaging a mutual passion is a rough, shitty thing to hear (regardless of whether it's true or absolutely false). My suggestion is to think about where they're coming from, and what the resentment is really targeted toward. Shift blame into exploration, and ask him if he's willing to take a step back and look at how your publishing hobbies co-exist and how they can co-evolve.

    \n\nMalcolm Sheppard \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nNovember 2007 \n edited November 2007
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n I'm not sure you want honest answers, but here they are anyway.\n
    Posted By: Ogremarco
    So, if I'm not willing to come up with a business plan and sink my savings into a publishing company i might as well not bother?
    \nYes to the first and no to the second. Littering OBS with the corpse of a half-baked vanity outfit that adds lines to search results doesn't help you or anyone working at the same scale as you. You don't need to spend a lot of money and "sink your savings," but write a fucking business plan.\n
    I have to risk my livlihood to be taken seriously?
    \nDepends. No for your output, yes for your pronouncements on game design and development as a professional field.\n

    I'm a bad person who's diluting the market with crap if i'm not going to suck some industry guy's dick for a writing gig?
    \nNo. Then again, equating a well-written submission with knob-gobbling is kind of screwy.\n
    Wanting to write a game as a hobby and just break even and be pleasantly surprised when I get some beer and con money is a spurious way to go about it?
    \nNo. Working at an effective loss and claiming you've succeeded because you can suck some money out of the till and the end of each day at the con and pay for hot dogs and beer, then claiming great financial success that will show the Man what for is somewhat dickish, however.\n
    I'm not serious unless I take a huge financial risk to start a company and hope I get everything right and hope no one i work with drops the ball so I don't go bankrupt?
    \nNo, you don't need to take big risks. You should, however, either pay anyone you work with a worthwhile rate for their labour flat, or offer them a share of the profits for proportional risk, but proportional reward. You should not abuse friendship and denigrate the value of labour to get people do do stuff for you cheaply, because it's unethical.\n
    or else i'm not serious if I don't bust my ass to sell myself to someone with money so i can either write their game or write my game with them looking over my shoulder?
    \nAs a general rule, nobody's interested in publishing your game except for you, so #2 will never happen. I will look over your shoulder for you for a small fee, as I did for Committee for the Exploration of Mysteries. You can ask Eric Boyd about that process. You should, however, bust your ass, since that increases the probability of producing anything worth buying.\n
    And small games are diluting the market?
    \nIf they're ill-conceived, yes. \n
    How exactly does a self produced game dilute the market?
    \nBy burying small press games that should stand out within an ever increasing number of entries that attach to press release feeds, blog aggregations, searches and e-commerce indices. Good small games do not emit telepathic quality rays that direct consumers to the right place, and promotional efforts shouldn't be the sole avenue for sales. It's important to accommodate casual browsers, people looking to round out a multiple product order, etc. There's nothing wrong with adding *good* work to that mass, but crappy stuff is a problem. Taste is subjective, but there are well-defined steps you can take to ensure that your game is not unbearably lousy.\n
    how does the tiny percent of a percent that my game might make really threaten the big guys?
    \nIt doesn't. It threatens other small guys. Big guys have their own dedicated categories/information streams and don't have this problem.\n
    Especally when the people buying probably wouldn't buy their product anyway because they're fed up with it?
    \nThey're not, as a rule. People have diverse tastes.
    \n
    \n

    \n I agree with Malcolm on a lot of points - that actually shaped several of the parameters of the Great Hundred project - but not on this one, which is pretty key:\n
    Posted By: Malcolm Sheppard
    How exactly does a self produced game dilute the market?
    \nBy burying small press games that should stand out within an ever increasing number of entries that attach to press release feeds, blog aggregations, searches and e-commerce indices. Good small games do not emit telepathic quality rays that direct consumers to the right place, and promotional efforts shouldn't be the sole avenue for sales. It's important to accommodate casual browsers, people looking to round out a multiple product order, etc. There's nothing wrong with adding *good* work to that mass, but crappy stuff is a problem. Taste is subjective, but there are well-defined steps you can take to ensure that your game is not unbearably lousy.
    The industry has a low cost of entry. That's not going to change - you can either accept it and find ways to differentiate yourself or not participate. Telling people to go home just makes enemies and it doesn't stop anyone from playing around with a hobby product. If you're telling a vast number of people that they don't belong, it's time to take a real hard look in the mirror.

    \n Recommended business plan for DIY games. #2 is crucial.

    1. start writing game.
    2. continue working at lucrative day job.
    3. finish writing game.
    4. playtest game.
    5. Edit game based on playtest.
    6. Get a bit of art for game.
    7. Slap price tag on game.
    8. Make game available for sale.
    9. Buy beer with proceeds.

    \n\nJ_Walton \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nNovember 2007 \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n It's like the X-Men: people fear what they do not understand.

    Are small games diluting the market? Meaning that there are less fans for each specific game line and more people who are fans of games -- in general -- who buy things here and there but don't subscribe to entire game lines? Meaning that individual products are selling fewer copies as the same purchasing dollars are distributed among more and more creators, since the audience isn't expanding at the same rate as new games are being published? So... we're all small scale hobby publishers. Um... great.

    Malcolm's point that "good" indie games compete with "bad" indie games for fans and dollars... I don't really know how to make that determination and never have. But indie publishing is not something that is -- or should be -- limited to a certain kind of product, especially one that meets an arbitrary standard like "good." Indie publishing is a Hundred Flowers Blooming environment. You're gonna like some stuff and not like other stuff. That's just the nature of things. And, yeah, sometimes you have to work to be noticed and sometimes great games, for whatever reason, don't get a whole lot of attention. But perseverance matters and I think, perhaps naively, that if your game regularly delivers a great play experience... people will find it, especially if you're demoing it at cons and playing with with other creators who will spread the word about the awesomeness of your product. Actively sharing your game through organizing play of it is pretty much a surefire way of showing people what it does and getting others excited about it.
    \n

    Posted By: JDCorleyAnyone who says anything about the RPG market doesn't know what they're talking about because nobody who knows anything about it has been paid a lot of money to study it for a long time.
    \nThis might be a first, but: I 100% agree with every word Jason wrote

    I'm not responsible for other publishers who feel that I "dilute" their market. They need to account for that in their own fucking business plan, if they care. Build up a brand. Make a name for yourself. And voila, you don't have to worry about what everybody else is doing anymore. The only people who need to worry about dilution are those who can't establish themselves as a known entity.

    As for me, I'm in the "get my investment back and maybe some money to spend on other cool indie games" niche. I'm perfectly happy with that. Seeing a sale means much less to me than seeing someone on rpg.net list Beast Hunters as one of their Top 5 indie games. Not because of fame or ego or whatnot, but because damn, we made something other people enjoy, and how cool is that? It's putting something positive out into the world.

    \n\nJDCorley \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nNovember 2007 \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    Posted By: Filip LuszczykBut do you actually need a business plan for something you don't intend to sell for money? Can't you just, well, write your game and don't care, and then move on to the next?
    \nA business plan doesn't have to have glossy covers and comb binding. (But that makes some of us feel cool, I have to admit, myself included.) All it is is some idea, some plan for "what am I doing, how long is it taking me, what are my milestones, where and in what form will I distribute it, how much will I ask for it, and what will I do with the money". It's just a tiny bit more than a household budget. And putting it in writing will keep you from floundering around and wasting your own time. What, just because you're a hobbyist, you have infinite time to flounder around in your own urine? Hell no, you want something to make your hobby work. And you have to plan that.
    \n

    \n\nChristian_Griffen \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nNovember 2007 \n edited November 2007
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n Here's a business plan for making back your investment:

    1. Figure out your budget for art, layout, and other services that you feel you need other people to do.

    1.a. Make it an amount you can bear to lose completely if things go to hell.

    2. Figure out from that what profit margin you want to have in order to recoup your investment in x copies sold. Add a couple of review copies as additional expenses there, if you want. Set the price of your product appropriately.

    3. Put the thing up on Lulu, and/or as PDF on rpgnow.com. Watch it make back your investment over time.

    4. To speed things up, send out review copies, run some demos, talk about your game online (you're passionate about it anyway, right?).

    That's it. After some time, if you keep making a sale here and there, your investment will be recouped. You could have print runs and join IPR or other places, but that would severely raise the initial investment, so if you're doing this to get your game out and just get your investment back, you need to figure out if that extra expense still complies with 1.a.

    ETA: Of course, this omits all of the "make and playtest the game" stuff that Matt W. already listed. They're still part of this
    \n
    \n
    \n

    Posted By: Matt WilsonRecommended business plan for DIY games. #2 is crucial.

    1. start writing game.
    2. continue working at lucrative day job.
    3. finish writing game.
    4. playtest game.
    5. Edit game based on playtest.
    6. Get a bit of art for game.
    7. Slap price tag on game.
    8. Make game available for sale.
    9. Buy beer with proceeds.
    \nSounds like a 10-year business plan to me

    And as for #9, I say buy a good beer even if you have to come out of pocket for half. Buying cheap bad beer dilutes the beer market.

    Posted By: Filip LuszczykActually, this whole business plan thing makes me wonder.

    For some strange reason, general discussions about game design and publishing often drift to the subject of money. Despite there allegedly not being any in it.

    But do you actually need a business plan for something you don't intend to sell for money? Can't you just, well, write your game and don't care, and then move on to the next?
    \nYou don't need anything. But making a business plan can be extremely valuable for any endeavor. Its just making a plan and planning has some value right?

    I'd think that a small press publisher could probably probably get good value out of a business plan that was 5-10 pages in length if it was done well. You might not need more more than 10 pages even if you were out looking for capital, depending upon the investment sources you were talking to (though you might to beef up the market analysis and feasability sections a bit).

    \n
    \n Here's the part where I come in, say some stuff which is true, and piss a bunch of people off.

    First off all -- there's a big difference between designing a game and being a publisher. Figure out which one you want. You don't have to be one to be the other. Ask yourself this: if your core desire is to put a game out that people will enjoy, then why does *selling it* matter to you? If the publishing part of it is your focus, then why not actually be professional about it? (And yes, that means investment, a business plan, etc.)

    To directly address Mr. Corley:\n
    Posted By: JDCorleyAnyone who says anything about the RPG market doesn't know what they're talking about because nobody who knows anything about it has been paid a lot of money to study it for a long time.
    \nPatentedly false, and oft-repeated by amateurs who want to feel better about themselves. Sorry, but that's the truth.

    I've worked in this industry since 1989. I've also been paid as a business consultant within it....and there are people in the business with even more experience and more industry-related marketing cred than me. Like it or not, there are people who know things that you don't. Dismissing that is simple arrogance, and serves no purpose other than to make people who don't know the ins-and-outs of the business feel like they're not missing anything critical. Unfortunately, that's bullshit -- and the history of this business is littered with wise-asses who thought that "nobody knows anything about it" and that they had as good a shot as anyone else.

    Game Design is game design -- and the self-defined "Indie" folks have, in many cases, proven their talents in those regards. But publishing is not game design, and the majority of indie publishers.....well, they make great game designers. (There are exceptions, obviously).

    Small games *do* dilute the market. It's a simple fact. However, if you're a good publisher, you don't really worry about the dilution, because you concentrate on a Long Tail strategy, and brand-building. But you can't deny the facts -- more stuff (both good and bad) means that each bit of stuff has to work harder to find an audience.
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
  • \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n\nJDCorley \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nNovember 2007 \n edited November 2007
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n The plural of anecdote is not data, so I am still right. A comprehensive analysis of the RPG market would not just be the "accumulated wisdom of experience" because then two people with identical experience could have very different analyses based on whether they are smart or stupid. (Or, you know, that they just had different perceptions, but what are the odds that one wouldn't be stupider than the other?!)

    A substantive analysis would have numbers in it, and margins of error, and confidence intervals, and it would be peer-reviewed, and expensive.

    Maybe saying that nothing is known was too extreme. I'll cop to that.

    Edit: And WTF, how does saying "nobody knows anything substantive" lead to "therefore you have as good a shot as anyone?" I think it would lead to the opposite conclusion, that the further you stray from established successes, the more risk you're taking. Just saying that nobody's ever done any thorough, broad analysis backed by systematically obtained data doesn't mean that there aren't good ideas and bad ideas.

    Posted By: Ryan MacklinActually, I would buy this if the following was true: "Every game -- big or small, indie or 'trad' -- dilutes the market." I cannot see how the format or style of the game has any further effect on the market.
    \n1) I didn't bring up "indie" vs "trad" -- so please let's keep that particular hornet's nest out of this, neh?

    2) Big games do tend to dilute as well -- but at the scale you're operating on at that level, the effect isn't really noticeable. They're big, right? Big = far easier to notice, kinda by definition..... [EDIT] To make it even more clear -- what I'm saying here is that in the "small game" end of the pool, the dilution has a much more critical negative effect, simply by virtue of the fact (to state it in the abstract) that it's hard to pick out 1 small thing from a field of 10,000 small things....but if you add 1 big thing to a field of 10 big things, you can still differentiate fairly easily.

      Posted By: Ryan MacklinEvery game -- big or small, 'indie' or 'trad', shitty or stunning -- dilutes the market.
      \nIsn't every game -- big or small, indie or trade -- just part of the market?

      "Dilute" implies that there is (at least) two solutes in the "market solution."

      If the distinction is small games and big games, as Sarka is disavowing the indie/trad distinction, then are we saying that "small games dilute the market" means "the introduction of more small games decreases the ratio of big games to small games?" Cause that's... not really saying much.

      If the distinction is good games versus bad games, as the connotation certainly implies, then the statement would be saying "the introduction of more small games decreases the ratio of good games to bad games" and would carry a conclusion (or assumption) that the ratio of good to bad is lower in small games than it is in big games.

      If the distinction is my games versus everybody else's games, which I think is what most folks who espouse the line are bitching about, the statement is "the introduction of more small games decreases the visibility of my games." To which the response is: "Yes. Maybe you should do something about that and differentiate your product."

      Markets are not sacred things that must be protected and nurtured. The gaming market is not some holy relic that we all as good gamers ought to maintain the purity of. The gaming market will be just fine with or without your or my participation in it. We benefit from it, not the other way around.

      Posted By: JuddHow do we access this wisdom that is all around us by people in the industry and those with marketing cred?

      How can we share their wisdom?
      \nI'm not sure what you're going for here, because the obvious answer makes me sound a bit snarky: Ask. That's the first step.

      Keep in mind though, that the sort 'wisdom' you're talking about is a commodity that is worth paying for -- and I fully admit that I'm saying that as a paid consultant. But I'd be saying it even if I wasn't. If I was starting, for example, a company that produces gadgets, I wouldn't expect to get free advice from someone with extensive experience in the gadget market -- and really, the rules shouldn't change just because we're "only" talking about games.

      So, yeah -- Ask. Generally speaking, you can probably expect a little bit of freebie advice, just on fellow-gamer goodwill alone. But to really pick someone's brain, you should be willing to pay them.

      In my experience, though -- the ones that are most in need of the help are the ones least likely to want to pay for it. *Shrug* It is what it is.

      \n Okay, I'm asking.

      Let's talk about a fantasy world.

      \n I'll try to answer everybody at once, so forgive the lack of blockquoting.

      What I'm getting at here is that the only reason to become a game publisher is if you want to run a publishing business. Game publishing is not the same thing as game designing. Being a publisher means running a business. It means creating a business plan, designing a website, coordinating art, editing, and layout, marketing the product, keeping finances in order, hiring and liaison with freelancers, coordinating distribution (whether you're talking about three-tier or direct), and so on. What it leaves little time for is actually designing games.

      It seems to me that a lot of gamers conflate the designing-a-game side of things with the publishing-a-game side of things....but I doubt any of them have said to themselves "Oh boy! I want to be a small-press publishing house!" No, they've said "I want to design games!"

      What I have yet to hear from anyone in the "fuck 'em in the ear! Put your game out there!" crowd is why, exactly, they have to enter the market and sell their game, to get it out there. If the focus of your interest is in the design, why would you spend time in dabbling as a publisher, rather than just designing games and offering them for free?

      If, as I suspect, the answer is "I deserve something for my efforts".....well, then, you need to get some business skills, and launch a publishing business (with the knowledge that you're not going to get a lot of design in day-to-day), or you need to find an exisitng publisher to release your game and pay you.

      Posted By: GMSkarkawhy, exactly, they have to enter the market and sell their game, to get it out there. If the focus of your interest is in the design, why would you spend time in dabbling as a publisher, rather than just designing games and offering them for free?
      \nHere's my reason. Our complete game, as Lisa and I envisioned it, needed art. It also needed good layout and editing to be fully realized. I had already put the rules up on the Forge, so if anyone wanted to, they could play the game for free. And now we've got a demo that gives away all of the conflict resolution rules.

      But the final game, with art and the beast tattoos, is bigger than just the text.

      The publishing goal, therefore, was to make back the money for the art and layout so that we could afford to possibly do the same thing again next time around.

      If I believed that art and layout didn't add anything to a game and that it's all just about the rules, I'd have put it out for free. But I don't. I'm happy we went the way we did.

      \n I'll give you my answer: Because I'm interested in learning more about the publishing side of things, so I'm dabbling in it. I'm treating running a business as a hobby & expierment, not a livelihood. I deserve something for my efforts, sure, but for me that's not money from others -- it's satisfaction from myself. And if people don't like that that's my intent, I'll happily not sell to them pure and simple. Hell, my first book is something 100% for charity and I'm giving refunds to those who bought my ashcan (though I should probably post about that), so I'm really not in it for the money.

      As for the "Fuck 'em in the ear" comment, the way I say it wasn't "Put your game out there!" but "Don't let someone else berate you and devalue you" like the OP talked about. Maybe Ben, who said that phrase in this thread, saw it as different, but there you go.

      I will say that a lot of people confuse "publishing" with "producing a physical product and asking money for it," and we as a community pay lip service to that but don't stress it enough. "Publishing" can also mean making a PDF and putting it on a website for free, or printing up rules, stapling them together and handing them out to friends. Hell, at this point I think I'm going to look more into that for some of my more obscure projects, because I don't want to go through the effort of selling them. If that's still "diluting" a market, fuck it; if free content on the Internet threatens a product or dilutes a market, that product or market is unworthy of existence.

      \n I'm more than happy being a lemonade stand to the food service industry.

      I think there's no conflict here.

      Posted By: Malcolm Sheppard

      You have an ethical duty to neither produce shit nor pay people shit when you are producing something for money.
      \nWhenever I've worked with an artist, I've sat down (last time it was lunch and I paid) told them what my budget was and asked what they could draw for that much money. We bartered back and forth, figured out how to make the book look pretty and that was that.

      Posted By: Matt_SnyderChristian, right on.

      The idea that indie publishers are makingunethicaldecisions by not paying actual living wages to creators is just silly. It It's entitlement crap. I don't owe anybody the "right" to make a living creating gaming stuff. I don't owe it to anyone whochoosesto eek out a living in the RPG industry to back off my financial decisions so they can buy ramen. Fuck them right in the ear, since there's a lot of that going around. (This is like safe sex on Family Guy!)

      Also, the idea that indie products might "dilute" the industry is just pitiful. Jesus Jump Up Christ, could there be any louder sign that things are fucked up in the industry? I mean, holy crap, you can't compete with John Q Nerd's Lulu Extravaganza to the point it's "diluting" your market? The mind reels.
      \n1) It sure is entitlement. I think artists and writers and other workers are entitled to make decent money and that wrapping yourself in a small press/"indie" flag to justify ripping workers off is pathetic. If you are a publisher, the risk of making nothing is yours. If you want to share that risk, then morality compels you to share the rewards, too. But you think it's just fine to pay people shit and then give them nothing if *you* make good bank out of it.

      That's called exploitation. Indulging in it makes you an unethical shitheel, and nobody should buy anything produced by an unethical shitheel.

      2) There is no difference between good and bad products on an electronic storefront. None.

      \n Judd, I think there IS a conflict here, but it's not clear.

      The conflict is this: People who make their living -- as in pay for heat and food and medical insurance and shit -- are, somewhat understandably, defensive about their profession. I think there's lots and lots of evidence that, yep, Gareth and Malcolm know a lot of stuff about the profession, and that they could teach a lot of stuff to literally everyone participating here and on SG.

      But, at the same time, they're invested in setting up a false dichotomy. Either you're a fucking lemonade stand goober and offer your shit for free, or you're one of us and make "real" money and a living. And if you're not one of us, you don't fucking know! so stop taking food from our table!\n
      "Here's the part where I come in, say some stuff which is true, and piss a bunch of people off."
      \nAh, yes, I remember. This is the place where people piled on poor Gareth for speaking truth about the underbelly of people selling things they're enthused about.

      Gareth, you're not just serving truth to us lemonde-selling schmucks, as you want to claim. You're swinging your dick around, yet again, showing how brilliant you are, and then blaming everyone else for your shit-in-the-neighbor's-yard behavior online. Have a conversation already! Stop talking at people and start talking with them. Which is precisely, precisely what Andy freaked out on you for the last fucking time. Come down of the pedestal and get the sneering assumptions out of the way. Stop playing the "Oh, you guys just treat me bad" card and start owning up to you parading your know-how around like it's some kind of badge or cred.

      For starters, I think you should seriously reconsider the dichotomy I explained previously aboud amateur and free and pay and fully employed. I don't buy it.

      Posted By: Joshua BishopRobyPosted By: Ryan MacklinEvery game -- big or small, 'indie' or 'trad', shitty or stunning -- dilutes the market.
      \nIsn't every game -- big or small, indie or trade -- just part of the market?

      "Dilute" implies that there is (at least) two solutes in the "market solution."

      It;s a metaphor, not actual chemistry.\n

      If the distinction is small games and big games, as Sarka is disavowing the indie/trad distinction, then are we saying that "small games dilute the market" means "the introduction of more small games decreases the ratio of big games to small games?" Cause that's... not really saying much.

      If the distinction isgoodgames versusbadgames, as the connotation certainly implies, then the statement would be saying "the introduction of more small games decreases the ratio of good games to bad games" and would carry a conclusion (or assumption) that the ratio of good to bad is lower in small games than it is in big games.
      \nIt's about products that meet minimum editorial and aesthetic standards, where hired labour is either paid fairly or offered a share of the lifetime revenue of the product, versus incoherently written converted Word docs that take up space without benefiting anyone.\n

      If the distinction ismy gamesversuseverybody else's games, which I think is what most folks who espouse the line are bitching about, the statement is "the introduction of more small games decreases the visibility of my games." To which the response is: "Yes. Maybe you should do something about that and differentiate your product."
      \nThe avenues that exist to do so represent a fraction of the possible ways to introduce the product to consumers. Electronic storefronts, aggregators and catalogs don't have inherent quality control.\n
      Markets are not sacred things that must be protected and nurtured. The gaming market is not some holy relic that we all as good gamers ought to maintain the purity of. The gaming market will be just fine with or without your or my participation in it. We benefit from it, not the other way around.
      \nI don't think it's "sacred." I think it's about minimal ethical responsibilities.

      \n\nChristian_Griffen \n\n\n

      \n
      \n 1. Nobody is forcing artists to work for low pay. They choose to do the work. They choose to make their living the way they want to, and they choose their projects. If we put them in a position where they had no choice about it, it would be our responsibility. But we are not.

      2. Actually, thanks to indie publishers, there are MORE jobs for artists out there in the RPG field than ever before. Joanna Barnum, who did the art for Beast Hunters, would probably have never been hired by a large RPG publisher; her style is not typical-RPG-enough. Plus, if she would have been, our work for her would STILL have been ADDITIONAL work--she could have done our work AND Wizard of the Coasts, or chosen not to do ours if Wizard keeps her busy enough. The pure idea that we're forcing poorly-paid jobs on artists because we are offering ADDITIONAL opportunities is utterly nonsensical. We are not in any position to rival a big publisher to the degree that we decrease the art they commission; that would be a preposterous suggestion, given our tiny market share. Every indie project is an ADDITIONAL opportunity for artists that they can feel free to accept or refust.

      3. Also, we paid Joanna exactly what she asked for in her very first proposal. We didn't even haggle. I think this is true for may indie publishers.

      4. There is an identifiable difference for customers between good and bad products on electronic storefronts: reviews, branding, and so on.

      \nI haven't quite gotten to the point of putting out my own game yet, but here's a couple of things making me want to try it:

      1. Having a shiny perfect-bound game book is neat, even if it comes from lulu.
      2. Participating in a booth and running demos at a con sounds like it could be fun.
      3. With rare exceptions (e.g., Risus) people seem to be much more likely to actually play a game that they actually bought, rather than one that was a free download.
      4. Some games (including some but not all of the ones I'm working on) work better when the presentation is there. (Hence, incidentally, I'm hoping Guild of Blades' plan to offer POD cards works out).

      Posted By: Malcolm SheppardPosted By: Ryan Stoughton
      The industry has a low cost of entry. That's not going to change - you can either accept it and find ways to differentiate yourself or not participate. Telling people to go home just makes enemies and it doesn't stop anyone from playing around with a hobby product. If you're telling a vast number of people that they don't belong, it's time to take a real hard look in the mirror.
      You have an ethical duty to neither produce shit nor pay people shit when you are producing something for money.The people producing these games aren't scam artists. There are people who want to do it for fun, and they can, because the market has a low cost of entry and willing buyers. They've got different ideas about what qualifies as a worthy product, and they're as entitled to their position as you are. Moral imperatives that have different interpretations depending on where you stand aren't going to help - you've got to adapt to that reality.

      Posted By: Joshua BishopRobyThe market is not the sole playground of "the professionals."
      \n....aaaaaaaaaaand we're done.

      Posts like this one lead me to realize that most of you have no idea what you're talking about, and that it's madness to try to speak about real concrete issues with folks whose definitions of reality are....somewhat flexible.

      For those who care, I'm using words that have actual real-world definitions -- and there is a specific reason why I use those words. It is not possible to converse on any level with folks who do not understand those definitions, or think they're loaded with "false dichotomies", or somehow don't fit their personal view of what the word means.

      All it's going to end up doing is pissing off and frustrating a bunch of people (myself included). We should stop.

      \n\nBen_Lehman \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2007 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n So, hey, I have a story about "diluting the market." It's a true story, it's about me.

      Let's see. It's Christmas of 2000. I'm an avid RPG player (I mean it, seriously, AVID) but I haven't bought a game book since ... two three years I think? Most of my games have come from used book stores, anyway. I'm not in touch with the market. I don't go to cons, I don't read about games online, I'm not in any way tied to the greater community.

      A friend of mine buys me a new game for Christmas. (Game will not be mentioned publically). I'm like "holy shit, NEW role-playing game?" I barely knew that they still made those. The color is really cool, too. It's super-exciting. I get very jazzed up about it and decide I'm going to run a one-shot on New Year's Eve. I pre-generate the characters to my player's specifications, I make a whole little scenario that showcases the cool setting without needing the players to know the world background, I do the whole shebang.

      New year's eve: We're playing, we're having fun, everything is great. Then ... we use the system to resolve something. Eh. It's flat and unexciting. Okay, whatever, we roll over it and keep playing and it's a good game. Then we come to a place where we need to use the extended conflict system ... and ... it doesn't work. Not as in "it doesn't give good results" it doesn't work. I did a back of the envelope calculation and figured out that it would have taken nearly 1000 repetitions of the resolution for it to have a decent chance of resolving. It's clear, abundantly clear, that this was never playtested. Not "never blind playtested." Not "never played in the context of a real game." But simply "the guy who wrote it never even sat down to try it out, and that was abundantly clear as soon as we did."

      We ended up handwaving it ... but the rest of the game was just ... flat. We spent the whole time avoiding conflict and danger because we didn't want to have to think about using that system anymore. Yuck.

      The whole experience soured me on new games. Why should I pay money for someone else's unplaytested crap? I mean, seriously, they clearly didn't even have the simplest concepts of game design, whereas I had a pretty together head about most of these things. I didn't buy any new games until Riddle of Steel in ... late 2002?

      If the game had been good, fun, exciting, I imagine I would have been buying a lot more games. It soured me on the concept of new role-playing games, and it wasn't until much later that I decided to give it a shot again.

      So there's something to that. The market can get diluted and flooded and even deflated with bad games.

      Which is totally different from "games that you bet your life savings on." The game in question was produced in large quantities and sold through distribution. I would put Polaris up against that game in terms of making customers excited any day of the week.

      yrs--
      --Ben
      \n

      \n\nDaztur \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2007 \n edited November 2007
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I think that POD has changed more stuff than most people realize. If it wasn't for POD I would have to pay for a small print run (of, what, maybe 500?) out of my own pocket before I saw even a cent and then pay through the nose mailing all my stuff out of Korea. It would be completely unworkable. If it weren't for POD I'd be MUCH better off doing to a publisher and just taking whever they give me.

      With POD its a whole other ball game. I can do layout since its something that I want to learn how to do anyway for my day job (curriculum development) and my co-author can do the art so we don't have to spend a dime. All we have to do is stick the thing on Lulu and there's zero risk and any money we get is pure profit. Zero risk money is always nice, even if there's not much of it.

      Of course factoring in opportunity costs the time we sink into this will surely make us less than minimum wage, but its fun so so what?

      Of course because of POD/PDFs there's a MASSIVE explositon of crappy RPGs (FATAL etc.) since there's no real filtering mechanism to keep that crap out that's built into the publishing system. But then to figure out which games are good I can just post "sell me on X" here or on rpg.net and get a pretty good idea if the same is decent or not. I find that "people on rpg.net like it" is a much better (although far from perfect) way of finding good games than "X publishing house published it."

      \n

      \n\nGeekGirlsRule \n\n\n

      \n Ok, here I am to throw in my two cents. I finally have a minute to come here and read enough of any given thread to comment intelligently.
      \n
      \nFirst, disclaimer: I am married to Ogre, however if you think this means I will blindly support and agree with him, you have very obviously never met me/us.
      \n
      \nPart of the problem here is that Ogre and I come from a very "punk rock" DIY sensibility, where if you're doing something solely for money, that makes it less pure to us. It's why when I went back to grad school after doing IT for two years, it was for Russian History and not Computer Science. Yeah, I'd be making a fuck of a lot more money with a CS degree, hell, I might even be working in my field with a CS degree, but that just feels dirty to me, because I don't love it. I do computer shit because it makes me money and I'm good at it, not because I'm particularly interested in it.
      \n
      \nAs for not paying artists "industry standard," this is how it goes: I ask and tell them what I can/am willing to pay them. Either they say yes or they say no. I'm not standing over anyone in an Ilsa She-Wolf of the SS costume beating them with a riding crop yelling, "Draw faster, swine!!! You will draw for what I offer you!!" Hell, with our circle of friends, most of the artists we know are more than willing to draw shit for us for dinner and a blow job, because the project sounds like fun or sounds interesting to them and because they love to draw/paint/whatever.
      \n
      \nAlso, speaking of industry standard, how do you feel about Dragon magazine's policies on buying fiction? Less than half the "industry standard" per word (as compared with other sci fi magazines/journals of comparable "cred"), plus you sign over your rights in perpetuity. No one else does that. That doesn't sound very fair, but people are still submitting because they want the publishing credit.
      \n
      \nAnd finally, no one has a "moral" obligation to put out a good product. For Heaven's sake, have you not looked at the movie ads for the last two decades. Good Lord, while I loved "Night of the Comet" in the 80s, it sure wasn't good. Pure capitalism has no morality, and was never intended as a moral system, Ayn Rand aside.
      \n

      Posted By: GeekGirlsRule
      As for not paying artists "industry standard," this is how it goes: I ask and tell them what I can/am willing to pay them. Either they say yes or they say no. I'm not standing over anyone in an Ilsa She-Wolf of the SS costume beating them with a riding crop yelling, "Draw faster, swine!!! You will draw for what I offer you!!"       \nWell yeah; alienated labour is easy to exploit. When people are convinced labour they enjoy is inaccessible unless they give it no economic value, they are amazingly cheap to hire. They *beg* to do it for cheap.

      And when you're their friend and you plan on making money off their work, you don't let them.\n
      Also, speaking of industry standard, how do you feel about Dragon magazine's policies on buying fiction? Less than half the "industry standard" per word (as compared with other sci fi magazines/journals of comparable "cred"), plus you sign over your rights in perpetuity. No one else does that. That doesn't sound very fair, but people are still submitting because they want the publishing credit.

Good Lord, while I loved "Night of the Comet" in the 80s, it sure wasn't good. Pure capitalism has no morality, and was never intended as a moral system, Ayn Rand aside.
      \nPure capitalism has no morality, but it is not a license to abandon morality. You do not magically have a license to rip people off and do other shitty things because it's The Market.

      Posted By: GMSkarkaPosted By: JuddSo, you tell me that there is this information out there.

      Cool.

      "Could you show me some?"

      "No."

      "Shit."

      Hence my frustration.

      If you can't share wisdom......
      \nThat's not it at all, Judd, and I'm frankly stunned that that's how you see it. As I told you in whisper, I'd be happy to provide you with information, but you're obviously wanting (nearly demanding, in fact) veryspecificinformation, but you're going about it by asking extremely wide-ranginggeneralquestions. I can't help if you do that -- there's no way for me to narrow it down. "What am I doing wrong" isn't something I can just jump in and magically solve for you, because I have no idea about your business, your skills, your set-up, your product, or, to put it even more simply, what you're doing -- wrong or otherwise.

      What, SPECIFICALLY, do you want to know?

      It wasn't about me, Gareth. It wasn't personal.

      There was this intimation that there are things that us amateurs just don't know. Things that we were getting wrong as publishers, even when our game designers came out alright.

      I was curious what mistakes you are seeing that lead you to say this:

      "I've worked in this industry since 1989. I've also been paid as a business consultant within it....and there are people in the business with even more experience and more industry-related marketing cred than me. Like it or not, there are people who know things that you don't. Dismissing that is simple arrogance, and serves no purpose other than to make people who don't know the ins-and-outs of the business feel like they're not missing anything critical. Unfortunately, that's bullshit -- and the history of this business is littered with wise-asses who thought that "nobody knows anything about it" and that they had as good a shot as anyone else."

      What common mistakes do you see? What mistakes are the wise-asses who this business is littered with make thinking that "nobody knows anything about it" making that causes them to litter whatever it is they litter?
      \n

      \n
      Posted By: Judd What common mistakes do you see? What mistakes are the wise-asses who this business is littered with make thinking that "nobody knows anything about it" making that causes them to litter whatever it is they litter?
      \nWow, you really don't understand. I must be doing an awful job of explaining....well, anything.

      Again, you're asking for a HUGE response. If you want a generalized answer (which would be so generalized as to be pretty much useless as advice), it is this: Amateurs get into publishing without understanding everything that running a publishing business entails. They end up being tripped up by any number of a thousand different things that they can get wrong, and often end up with a project that they sunk money into, which they lost money on, and which nobody remembers. In the end, all they did was clutter the market, making it harder for other folks just like them (who did the same thing, with the same result, etc. -- leading to a big vicious cycle.).

      My statement that you quoted was a very specific response to a claim (made by JDCorely) that "anyone who says anything about the RPG market doesn't know what they're talking about", which is extremely wrong. So, I pointed out that it was wrong, and why dismissing that was arrogance. I don't know how much more specific that needs to be.

      From what it looks like from here, you're asking me to tell you what mistakes are being made, specifically. I can't tell you that, because it's not that simple. Publisher A might not understand how to base his products' price correctly, and end up taking a loss on production because he didn't take hidden costs into account. Publisher B might not understand how distribution works, and ends up being taken advantage of by a less-than-ethical fulfillment house. Publisher C might produce his game in a format that has him making a certain amount of money at a certain level of popularity, but which ends up *losing* him money if he has to start shipping in higher numbers if it gets popular....there are thousands of different stories, and it all comes down to not understanding the business.

      I hope that I'm making myself clear enough.
      \n
      \n
      \n

      Posted By: Ben Lehman
      So there's something to that. The market can get diluted and flooded and even deflated with bad games.
      \nYep, I fully agree. And I think (certainly I hope) Malcolm would agree that it doesn't inherently have anything to do with small vs. big games. TSR diluted the market with shitty D&D products and drove their company to collapse; publishers with stars in their eyes diluted the market with shitty d20 products and sank the market for everyone.

      I do think that everytime someone publishes a game that isn't edited, isn't playtested, is pug-fugly, whatever, it hurts almost every other small independent game -- not Dogs or Polaris, but all the games that are good and worth buying but don't get the exposure of being in the indie scene's top 5. If the scene wants to be self-sustaining and to help everyone who can write a good game succeed, it should work to discourage the publication of lousy games.

      The same way, it doesn't reflect well on the scene that there's so little accessible advice about how to ethically employ people for the services your game needs but you can't provide yourself. I don't think that most people who have published their own games and post to SG have taken advantage of artists and editors -- but my perception is that, a lot of the time, the tossed off advice is "get your friends to do it for free!"
      \n

      \n

      \n\nDecember 2007 \n

      \n
      \n
      Posted By: charlequinTSR diluted the market with shitty D&D products and drove their company to collapse; publishers with stars in their eyes diluted the market with shitty d20 products and sank the market for everyone.
      \nI'm sure Matt will say that I'm using my industry experience to "swing my dick around" or something, but both of those are partially incorrect --

      Yes, TSR diluted the market (at one point having something like 6 different campaign settings available, for example), but it was largely a lack of inventory control, coupled with a horrible year for fiction releases leading to a massive book return that they weren't ready to absorb (although I doubt anyone would have been ready -- it was over a million copies returned as unsold in 1996 alone). This lead to massive debt, and that ended the company.

      The Publishers were only partially to blame for the d20 crash -- you can't really blame them though, because there have always been some publishers producing shitty product, d20 or not. The problem was undiscerning distributors and retailers, who bought anything with d20 on it, because it was viewed as "free money" -- instant sales. It wasn't. Good products sold, bad products stacked up. Distributors and Retailers kept buying anyway, until the bad product stack grew noticeable and THEN they got gunshy and stopped ordering.
      \n

      Posted By: GMSkarka

      From what it looks like from here, you're asking me to tell you what mistakes are being made, specifically. I can't tell you that, because it's not that simple. Publisher C might produce his game in a format that has him making a certain amount of money at a certain level of popularity, but which ends up *losing* him money if he has to start shipping in higher numbers if it gets popular....there are thousands of different stories, and it all comes down to not understanding the business.

      I hope that I'm making myself clear enough.
      \nAlright, Gareth, it is beginning to feel like that we are beginning to talk, rather than posture on our corners.

      What is interesting to me is the topics you've listed above are all things we've talked about in countless threads, talked about at cons and over lunch and over IM. These are issues on our minds, without a doubt.

      We are amateurs but we're a community of amateurs and a community of autodidacts. I'm sure there is stuff for us to learn.

      And there is this bullshit divide that I don't quite understand. Rather than the thought that we are all designing games, there's this thought that some are over here because they pay their rent with it and some are over there because they still have day-jobs.

      Because when a gamer sits down at the table with the game, it doesn't matter two fucks what the publisher did with their money. What matters is that there is a good game, I'd think.

      I'm just confused by the whole way this went down. This was difficult and tiring.

      Posted By: GMSkarkaI hope that I'm making myself clear enough.
      \nSee, this is stuff I'd be interested in hearing you go on about. In a column, or a podcast, or a...

      Well, fuck, man, take your pick of "my show" venues. At whatever length you want. I'm there.

      But this, being a forum, and an ongoing discussion seems like a terrible place to try and speak from that experience at the length and in the depth you feel you'd need, precisely because it's impossible to get across the stuff you want to, and don't have catchy reference material to point to.

      So, what I'm suggesting is this:

      Chances are, it'd make the "stuff to watch" list here (if is was engaging, hell, I'd put it there in a heartbeat).       Posted By: charlequin

      Yep, I fully agree. And I think (certainly I hope) Malcolm would agree that it doesn'tinherentlyhave anything to do with small vs. big games. TSR diluted the market with shitty D&D products and drove their company to collapse; publishers with stars in their eyes diluted the market with shitty d20 products and sank the market for everyone.
      \nThat's exactly right. Remember that the basic interface for an online consumer and retailer is basically a time-sensitive index, with newer stuff in front. You might be led to a particular place by promotions or buzz, but browsing counts for a lot. The more you have to browse through crap the tougher it is.

      I may be rough sometimes, but keep in mind that I've also told Matt Sprange to suck my dick, so I'm not discriminating on the basis of company size.\n

      I do think that everytime someone publishes a game that isn't edited, isn't playtested, is pug-fugly, whatever, it hurts almost every other small independent game -- notDogsorPolaris, but all the games that are good and worth buying butdon'tget the exposure of being in the indie scene's top 5. If the scene wants to be self-sustaining and to help everyone who can write a good game succeed, it should work to discourage the publication of lousy games.
      \nI don't think that community standards are the best focus because there's are all kinds f interpersonal issues. I do think that there are workflow methods that *greatly* help with quality. Aeternal Legends was produced this way and from what I've gathered, Evil Hat's also getting into this kind of intensive critical collaboration.\n
      The same way, it doesn't reflect well on the scene that there's so little accessible advice about how to ethically employ people for the services your game needs but you can't provide yourself. I don't think that most people who have published their own games and post to SG have taken advantage of artists and editors -- but my perception is that, a lot of the time, the tossed off advice is "get your friends to do it for free!"
      \nI was in fact given this advice. I didn't take it. Keep in mind that I don't ascribe malicious intent here. People have the *opportunity* to pay badly because of bigger issues in creative fields that create the *expectation* of poor pay. Plus, the idea that the Market instead of the time and effort spent should determine arrangements is a destructive meme affecting the whole process.

      \n Malcolm, I think that your stance in all of this is really refreshing, as I understand it.

      The way I understand it is "Everyone has an obligation to release good work, which upholds ethical standards. Those standards might be different for every publisher, but there are certain things which sould be universal, including paying those who work on your products, and not taking anyone for granted."

      Does that sound about right? If I got that right, I agree 100%.

      Because what you're proposing is essentially a systemized way of respecting everyone involved. Respecting the customer by upholding standards. Respecting yourself by releasing the best product you can. Respect any co-creators/co-producers by giving them control over their contributions and paying them fairly.


      "Respect everyone involved" is a stance that is surprisingly rare.

Being able to say "This is an example of what I'm talking about, this is where a gap exists" is an important thing, especially if you're offering warnings/admonitions to publishers.

      \n Malcolm, you've pointed at Ron before as an indication that most indie games pay artists crappily. I really don't think that Ron's practices (whatever they are) are used by anyone but Ron. Every indie publisher does things differently. If you're mad at Ron, you should bring it up with Ron (he's not even on SG much, if at all). I keep numbers on every copy of Push I've sold and split profits evenly between all contributors, including my cover artist. When my cover artist's percentage of the profits reaches a point higher than the amount I paid her originally for the artwork (they haven't so far, but probably will soon), I'm going to cut her another check, even though she didn't ask for a percentage in the original agreement, because I decided, back when I was founding Push, that it's the right thing to do.

If you can get your stuff into a brick and mortar store then its different, but I don't see how you'd be able to get your stuff into a store without a good bit of buzz. Maybe I'm wrong and there's people who sift through big rpg sales sites looking for good stuff, but I don't think that's the sort of person you want to focus on for sales...

      From what I've been able to gather and my own experience, sales from browsers are actually substantial on the whole, but when divided between so many products is problematic when it comes to sales per company. The effect of having an entry on the front page of an OBS site, for example, is noticeable.

A business is a legally-recognized organizational entity, existing within an economically free country, designed to sell goods and/or services to consumers or other businesses, in an effort to generate profit.

      That's it. There's no "high-status" bullshit there. That's the definition of the word. If you're not in an effort to generate profit,you are not a business.Simple fact. No emotional context, no dominance games, no value judgements, beyond that simple fact.

      Posted By: GeekGirlsRuleAnd, sometimes, Malcolm, they're your friends and they're really into what you're doing and don't care if they get paid because they're having FUN.       \nWell, I think there's a lot to be said for fairly compensating people who do stuff for you. What constitutes "fair" might be a bone of contention of course. I do translation work for a living, and I fully expect to get paid a lot less for RPG-related stuff, and little to nothing for anime fan projects, especially compared to the work I do on mass-market video games. I do prioritize projects based on what'll pay the bills, but I'll still do lower-paying stuff to hone my skills or just because it's a project I think is fun. I've never in any way consider myself to have been cheated; rather, I've gotten opportunities to "donate" to things (like Tenra Bansho Zero) I have a personal interest in.

      The point being, I agree with Malcolm that we shouldn't be taking advantage of other creative types, but it helps to keep in mind that (1) "fair" compensation is relative to the medium and scale of the end product, and (2) those of us who are offering said services have a say in what constitutes adequate pay.

      \n I think Jonathan Walton's system for Push -- payments over sales -- is one good compromise. A split of the net is another. I think these should be for the life of the product. The contributor is agreeing to take a risk or settle for not much right away, but can benefit from success with something other than his/her name in a book. Paying up front is pricey, but for the publisher it can be lucrative over the long run, since you get pure gravy once the project pays for itself.

      \n There's also the very real part paying your dues pays in one's development in a creative field. I worked fifteen years before I even landed an agent, and with a few notable exceptions I did a lot of work during that fifteen-year period with no expectation of pay and (sure enough) no money.

      It's not a question of deserving to be paid. Everyone, writer or artist or onion-picker, deserves to get paid for honest labor. But deserve often doesn't enter into the business equation, for better or ill. Some people get paid, some don't, and the some that do are almost always those who've toiled for a long time to get into a position where payment comes.

      Posted By: shreyasI have a writer hat too. I'm responding *as* an artist in response to a writer's depiction of a situation I don't like.       \n\nSouthpaw \n\n\n

      \n
      \n Don't forget simple business sense: if it's not worth it to you, don't do it. No one twists any writer or artist's arm to work on a given project. So consider the benefit-to-effort ratio and decide if it's worth your time and effort to produce the material. To relate this back to the point of this thread: I quit writing roleplaying stuff when I realized the work going in didn't pay off enough in terms of cash or exposure to warrant continuing.

      The fact is that comics and RPGs are niche areas of production with very little money floating around for anybody to get paid. Somebody somewhere, probably a lot of somebodies, are going to do work without remuneration unless they're lucky enough to crack the top tier. Which is highly unlikely.

      \n\nDaniel_M_Perez \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2007 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n The problem that I am seeing here (well, at least one of them) is one of vocabulary, and I'm gonna suggest the following definitions to see if we can start actually getting somewhere.

      Simply put, there are publishers (small p) and Publishers (capital P): publishers are people who publish a game because they have done all the work so far and want to see it "out there" and beyond this (or perhaps any other game they create) they have little to no interest in pursuing publishing as a business pursuit; Publishers, on the other hand, are people who are explicitely engaged in the active pursuit of publication as a business, meaning that whether it is their game or not is irrelevant, putting out the game and making a profit from it is the primary raison d'etre. Now, I realize these definitions are a bit constrained, but indulge me.

      Note: any name I use as an example below is based solely on my perception based on what I know/how well I know the people involved. If I name you, please excuse me, especially if I misrepresent your position, in which case, call me out immediately so that I may fix my examples.

      Most folks here at SG who have a game out, from what I have seen and IMO, fall into the publisher camp: they had an idea, they wrote the game and wanted to see it out there, so they went through all the steps it takes to put together a finalized commercial-quality product and published it, but beyond that, they are not out to find other games to publish under their company name. I think everyone who publishes something has a vested interest in the financial well-being of that product, if only to recoup the initial costs and perhaps even fuel a later product, but beyond this, they are not terribly interested in pursuing the hardcore business strategy to get their game to produce as much profit as possible. So for example, you can have Judd publish Dictionary of Mu, and he wants to see the game out there, and he wants it to make back his investment, and now that it has, he think it's cool, especially because the profits from Mu most likely help out the initial investment for 1st Quest when it eventually gets out, but Judd isn't necessarily running Paka's Thread Games as a dedicated business pursuit, trying to get Mu into as many stores as possible, trying to maximize the profit potential of the book, building a brand that will eventually benefit anything else he publishes under that banner, and making financial decisions so that eventually, with a couple of books in its catalog, Paka's Thread Games can become a full-time profitable endavour. It's not that Judd doesn't think it would be awesome to have these things happen, and not that he doesn't pay attention to how well his book is doing or that he doesn't manage his finances in order to keep the book in print and supply demand, it's just that he's not in the business of being a Publisher; he's a game designer who published his game.

      Cont.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n\nDaniel_M_Perez \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2007 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Publishers, like Gareth, are in a different camp: they had an idea, they wrote the game and wanted to see it out there, so they went through all the steps it takes to put together a finalized commercial-quality product and published it, but then, after that, they decided that they were in for the long haul, and that they were going to run a publishing house; whether the next project was their own game or not (and in many cases it is, but it usually happens that you see that branching off), they were going to actively seek new projects to publish because publishing is a business pursuit for them, and without products coming out, the business cannot grow, sustain itself and eventually become profitable. Publishers (capital P) are pursuing a business with every move they make; they are looking for more than just a return on investment, they are actively seeking an expansion of their products and product lines, actively building brand recognition, actively managing finances that fuel one or two or three projects ahead, and actively seeking the growth of the company. So for example, you have Evil Hat, which started out with Fate, and then published Don't Rest Your Head and Spirit of the Century (and will eventually publish Dresden Files), and then it did something new in the "indie" field, it brought in Chad Underkoffler under its publishing wings, and officially became the new publisher of Atomic Sock Monkey Press products, doing the same as well with Daniel Solis's Do. I think with Evil Hat, and especially if you follow Fred's frequent posts on running the company, you can see very clearly the difference between publishing your book and running a publishing business; it's not that a publisher (small p) won't benefit from Fred's experience (quite the contrary, anyone who publishes a book can benefit from that kind of info), but a publisher is just not interested in taking those extra steps, in doing all that other work (because if they did, they would be Publishers), they're quite happy with their book being out there for folks to buy.

      Because advances in technology mean that the entry barrier to publishing a book are super low, we find that a lot of people can be publishers, but not everyone is a Publisher. And frankly, not everyone is cut out to be a Publisher either; some folks will be perfectly fine being a publisher and that's it. But just because one is a publisher does not mean one is a Publisher; the only thing they have in common is that both engage in the act of publishing a book.

      Does this all make sense?

      Posted By: Jonathan WaltonI really don't think that Ron's practices (whatever they are) are used by anyone but Ron. Every indie publisher does things differently.
      \nIsn't there a community responsibility to take a stance on how its members -- especially people with leadership positions within said community -- behave, though? It kind of bothered me last time this came up that there seemed to be very little "Yeah, Ron is behaving really unacceptably here" and a lot more of "well, Ron doesn't really represent anyone here...." The fact that no one seems to \n
      Posted By: Sam I Am
      It's not a question ofdeservingto be paid. Everyone, writer or artist or onion-picker, deserves to get paid for honest labor. Butdeserveoften doesn't enter into the business equation, for better or ill.
      \nThat seems like a really awful stance to take, obscuring responsibility using the passive voice. If you're a game designer who brings other people in to work on your project, you are an employer. The people who work on the project don't get paid by some nebulous capitalist force, they get paid by you. If the project isn't economically feasible once fair wages have been paid to those involved, then it should not be done, just like designer shoes should not be made if they can only be profitable with sweatshop labor.

      As Malcolm said earlier, there's a fair way to keep initial costs down in small-press creative projects like this by sharing both risk and reward with your employees: if everyone does work "for free" upfront and shares equally in the profits from sales, you're bringing your artists &c. in on the same model you (the publisher) are using: investing your labor for the hope of a monetary return. That's totally fine, and pretty much how I would expect most small-press projects like this to function.

      Posted By: charlequinPosted By: Sam I Am
      It's not a question ofdeservingto be paid. Everyone, writer or artist or onion-picker, deserves to get paid for honest labor. Butdeserveoften doesn't enter into the business equation, for better or ill.
      \nThat seems like a really awful stance to take[.]
      Like it or not, we don't live in "from each according to his ability, to each according to his need" society. People get paid if/when they get paid based on a case-by-case situation. As I said before deserve has nothing to do with it. A fair amount of creative media, from films to comics, are made by people taking little to nothing up front on the off chance that they'll make some many on the back end.

      Yes, everyone deserves to get paid, but sometimes they don't. That's the risk they take. If that's too much for you or anyone else, so be it, but the situation isn't going to change because of righteous indignation.

      Posted By: Sam I AmPeople get paid if/when they get paid based on a case-by-case situation. As I said beforedeservehas nothing to do with it.
      \nYou're dodging responsibility here -- for yourself or for others, since I don't know whether you are someone who employs others on publishing projects. You keep using these passive voice sentences as if pay-for-work is something that comes out of the aether. It's not -- it's something that comes from specific people acting in the role of employer.

      If you (this is the hypothetical "you" here) are an employer and you cannot afford to pay a fair wage to your employees, you have a moral responsibility not to undertake the project, or to alter it so that your wages can become fair. That's true of giant, transnational corporations (who are committing moral wrongs when they employ sweatshop labor or try to use legal loopholes to cheat employees of pay) and it's true of tiny independent publishing outfits. The fact that we live in a society in which immoral employment practices are common or encouraged does not make them moral, nor does it in any way reduce the moral imperative on employers to reject them in favor of fair pay.

      It's true that "righteous indignation" isn't going to fix this problem for the whole world, but we're not talking about the whole world -- just this one little hobby. Everyone who publishes an indie RPG is an individual person with a face and a name, and I'm not highly inclined to accept "well, that's just how the world works" as a justification for unfair practices from them.

      I'm really happy that a lot of people in here have stood up and said "I practice only fair pay practices with my artists/editors/what have you, and I don't know of anyone specifically who does otherwise."

      Posted By: JuddIn my eyes, the guy who makes a game and posts it on his blog is engaging in as valid a method of publishing as the POD publisher via lulu to the big print publisher who get's his books on the cheap from China to the many methods in between are just as valid and just as equal.

      There is no divide but in our heads. We're all trying to get games into people who will enjoy and get something out of said game's hands because we believe in it.

      What are we arguing about?
      \nI would say that while Publisher A (blog, free), Publisher B (POD), and Publisher C (China) are all involved in publishing, only B and C are possibly in the publishing industry -- the word "industry" implying a business orientation, involving costs, goods, and profit.

      The concern I think I see raised here with regard to "dilution of the industry" is when a publisher -- it can be A, B, or C -- is flooding the market (market, implying MONEY/BUSINESS/CUSTOMERS aka the publishing industry not FANBASE/AUDIENCE) with product.

      A vast amount of new product -- and this is no matter whether the product is actually good or bad -- dilutes the market, meaning the amount of material is greater than it was. This means that each individual product gets less attention from the CUSTOMERS. It's simply a question of big numbers here.

      Supplementary dilution effects related to Quality and Operating a Business:

      If this new product is of poor QUALITY, that poor quality product has ADDITIONAL NEGATIVE EFFECTS on the market (as noted above in the TSR/d20 glut issue) by reducing the number of CUSTOMERS who look at the vast amount of product, unhappily see more poor quality product than good quality product, and give up looking.

      If this new product is produced with no regard to BUSINESS OPERATIONS (i.e., with no interest in gaining profit, paying contributors a fair wage, pricing the product to the market), this means that even more such product can be produced, further increasing the overall amount of product, and overall increasing the amount of dilution.

      The discussions of:
      * "what *is* publishing?"
      * "only high quality products should be published (free or into the industry)."
      * "by giving away milk for free, you are putting the squeeze on dairies."

      are all separate discussions.

      I believe that they have become entangled in this discussion.

      I suggest you detangle them.

      CU

      \n I have a small interest in this issue as a consumer, but also as an amateur (visual) artist who has in the past received small moneys for work.

      Exploitation, as M. Sheppard reminds us, is Bad. But to engage in a capitalist venture means exploitation by definition. If you are making money, you are sucking it from somebody's labour; although it may be your own.

      The alternative to being exploited is not being exploited. You can not work, or perhaps work in a different field where you feel the exploitation less keenly.

      If some small-s publisher decides to use out-of-copyright art, or illustrate it himself with distinctly amateur drawings, instead of paying me less than a fair price for my work, my work goes unsold and I take a job on nightfill.

      I wonder, as a consumer, how one determines the exact proportion of value added by art and by text. Does really good art add something to my gaming experience? Possibly, but then I've enjoyed games without art or with very poor art. One learns to substitute.

      \n\nbankuei \n\n\n

      \n\nDecember 2007 \n edited December 2007
