      \nThis means that each individual product gets less attention from the CUSTOMERS. It's simply a question of big numbers here.

      While it's true each individual customer has a limited amount of money and time to devote to any product, I think it's crucial to recognize:

      a) Roleplaying games aren't just competing with each other, they're competing with all other forms of entertainment, and recently, there's a lot more entertainment that is interactive, social, and/or creative at the same time.

      b) Not all forms of distribution/exposure are equal. Indie games, are not pushing mainstream industry games off shelves. For the most part, they're put where the small press games have always been- off to the side as curiosity items, and at best, they might get their own section.

      If we're talking the internet, how many people start their journey into tabletop roleplaying with an internet search, instead of looking at the website on the back of the book they just bought? The major dilution they'll encounter in that, is numerous IRC/Forum games, and small press games would be a tiny, tiny fraction of that. Not to mention, it''s not like the industry ever had a serious lock on the internet to begin with, so there's no real dilution in that, either.

      c) The way in which the "industry" and "indie" networks of players do not overlap. Traditional mainstream companies have focused mostly on long term play. Customers will be looking for products that either their friends are playing, or will be easily convinced into playing (with the major issue being that learning the rules does make for a barrier of entry). (EDIT FOR EXAMPLE: Much like the way in which computer customers look for software for their current OS, not constantly comparing OS's unless they're very dissatisfied in the first place.)

      In that regard, indie games have only pulled away a marginal number of people, most of whom, were basically unsatisfied with the industry options already. We don't see diehard gamers of the mainstream games abandonding campaigns to play small press games, we see the disatisfied folks or the people who stopped playing years ago, or have never played a roleplaying game before making up the majority of the indie networks.

      With that, the two major differences are that the small press games have utilized a lot more options which are safer investments from a business standpoint and generally made play and building play networks the focus of a sustainable hobby. Were it simply numbers to succeed, WOTC would shit out the rpg equivalent of Cheapass Games and be done with it. Instead, people who buy D&D buy it because they either already are playing it, or know they can get friends together to play it, and hopefully they're having fun with it, not because it has 400 supplements to it.

      With all that in mind, the biggest effect that the small press has had on the traditional industry is providing valuable information on alternative business and play models they would not have normally gambled on. For example, Greg Stolze is using Lulu to print Reign, D&D 4E is using Actual Play reports to produce hype, and various companies have started either selling PDFs or at least using them as a medium for promotion.

      All in all, this whole thought of "dilution" seems to be based in a failure to examine the really crucial question- can you create a play network that has an engaging enough activity to compete with the online experience- whether WoW, threadwars in forums, or looking at porn?

      Ryan Dancey's recent thoughts on the numbers in rpg sales is pretty worth looking at too:

      http://www.therpgsite.com/forums/showpost.php?p=149735&postcount=160

      Chris

      \n\nMalcolm Sheppard \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2007 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n a) People have always had more things to do than time to do it in. The attached buzzwords are not especially important.

      b) Anybody who's had a chance to analyze OBS sales knows that people do in fact buy things after category-directed browsing and that vendor-indexed reviews don't matter much (and of course, the theoretical guy who gets into gaming via web browsing is not even the subject of this thread). An RPGNet review sometimes matters, but not much. Browsing is a huge source of direct sales. The larger the small press index, the more difficult browsing is, the less there is to go around and the more people inadvertently pick up crap. The metaphor of a store with shelves is pretty off, since these issues take place outside of and before actual brick and mortal get into it, and across multiple consumer and retail sales.

      Of course, there are times when shelf space does come into it. Take the Forge Booth at Gen Con 2006, which posted record sales -- but awful sales past the top tier. Dilution has already had an effect in the indie-brand corner.

      c) The idea that the "indie" and "mainstream" (not the skepti-quotes) have mutually exclusive fan bases is . . . off. It also panders to the worse excesses of dogmatic thinking to believe there's an US and THEM, but that's another discussion. The guys who only play one game are not even in the picture, and the ones that don't do not fit into pure categories, no matter how much RPGSite and Forge wankers alike love narratives about how someone has seen the light and doesn't play Bad Games any more. Of course, giving these narrative privilege is basically taking a big dump on everybody with flexible tastes, too.

      d) People have been using Actual Play as a promotional tool forever. Offhand, I remember reading about Immortals Rules play reports in White Dwarf when . . . well, back when WD actually would mention anything about that sort of thing. It's only become the Capitalized Term of Actual Play after it became apparent that a lot of people were talking instead of playing anything. This is still true to a certain extent (remember Jack Spenser Jr?), but the formal AP phenomenon is a response to a problem, not a straightforward positive development.

      The points of innovation lie with technologies that have changed publishing, which everyone has access to and used as soon as it became available. Mentioning this as a point of innovation is kind of like taking credit for the end of the Cold War because the Berlin Wall fell in your lifetime. You're welcome. Also, I invented the Internet.

      Posted By: bankueiThis means that each individual product gets less attention from the CUSTOMERS. It's simply a question of big numbers here.

      While it's true each individual customer has a limited amount of money and time to devote to any product, I think it's crucial to recognize:

      (lots of really good points snipped)
      \nI don't necessarily completely disagree with what you're saying here, Chris.

      If I may, allow me to expand/modify/respond to some of the things you're saying, to give the way I see each:\n
      Posted By: bankueib) Not all forms of distribution/exposure are equal. Indie games, are not pushing mainstream industry games off shelves. For the most part, they're put where the small press games have always been- off to the side as curiosity items, and at best, they might get their own section.
      \nAgreed, for the most part.

      However, consider that d20 publishing. d20 products are pushing/had pushed other d20 products off of shelves. That's the primary effect.

      Overall glut and dead inventory of one sector of product (say, d20 products) can squeeze all other gaming products off the shelves -- less GURPS books, less White Wolf, less side-shelf space for indie games (carried in distro/retail). That's the secondary effect. . . and may or may not have been minor.

      The tertiary effects, and perhaps as powerful as the primary one, are:
      A. the customer looks at the dead inventory which doesn't interest him or her, hasn't changed, and has sat on the same shelf for months or years, and loses interest in searching between the dead inventory for new inventory placed there.

      B. The retailer, seeing low sales of dead inventory (in this example, d20 products), determines that GAMING PRODUCTS aren't selling. Which is both true and untrue -- the dead inventory isn't selling, and is depressing sales of other stuff, because there's no room for other stuff on the physical shelf.

      Again, that's retail. I don't think it necessary directly disagrees with what you're saying.

      Posted By: bankueiIf we're talking the internet, how many people start their journey into tabletop roleplaying with an internet search, instead of looking at the website on the back of the book they just bought? The major dilution they'll encounter in that, is numerous IRC/Forum games, and small press games would be a tiny, tiny fraction of that. Not to mention, it''s not like the industry ever had a serious lock on the internet to begin with, so there's no real dilution in that, either.
      \nI don't think we're talking about NEW GAMING CUSTOMER here. I think we're talking about REGULAR GAMING CUSTOMER -- and more than that: a REGULAR GAMING CUSTOMER WHO BUYS VARIED PRODUCTS.

      That's the customer that any sort of expansion/dilution of the market sidetracks.

      See below for more.\n
      Posted By: bankueic) The way in which the "industry" and "indie" networks of players do not overlap. Traditional mainstream companies have focused mostly on long term play. Customers will be looking for products that either their friends are playing, or will be easily convinced into playing (with the major issue being that learning the rules does make for a barrier of entry).

      (snip)

      In that regard, indie games have only pulled away a marginal number of people, most of whom, were basically unsatisfied with the industry options already. We don't see diehard gamers of the mainstream games abandonding campaigns to play small press games, we see the disatisfied folks or the people who stopped playing years ago, or have never played a roleplaying game before making up the majority of the indie networks.
      \nI disagree that it's a pulling away, or that people buying indie games are necessarily unsatisfied with the mainstream. I personally know a fairly large number of people who play both mainstream and indie games. Playing isn't zero-sum, but purchasing might be.

      I could be wrong, here. I only know the demographics of the gamers I personally know/read.

      That being said, yes, there are customers who focus on one "slice" of the market to the exclusion of the rest of the market. That could be "only WotC products" or "only d20 products" or "only d20 products produced by Company X" or "just White Wolf products" or "just Exalted products" or "just indie games" or "just indie games made by people I know" or any other criteria you care to name.

      The narrower the customer's slice, the less impact market dilution has. If you only buy WoD books, a million d20 products on your shops physical or virtual shelves makes no difference, except if they physically (in real world) or visually (online) crowd the WoD product you're looking for out of view -- the minor secondary factor mention above, which leads to the tertiary factor.

      Say that all of the WoD product gets pushed off of the front page of an online shop, in favor of, hell, let's say FGU product (mystically, FGU has a sudden renaissance). The casual customer looks at the page, sees all FGU and no WoD, and keeps on passing by. (However, a WoD customer with a history of buying from said shop and the drive to continue a page in is unaffected by this -- they are motivated enough to know where to look and keep looking, and they effectively ignore the FGU material.)

      The wide-slice folks -- in my experience, folks who are fans of games in general, be they mainstream or indie or board or card or whatever -- are often very casual customers when presented with the market. Thus dilution/expansion affects them.

      Indeed, I think that's true even for a wide-slice customer who comes to the store/shop looking for a specific game they're heard the buzz on (say, Beast Hunters). If they fail to find a copy of BH (and, if they're particularly flush with cash at the moment, possibly even if they do acquire it), many will look for "what else in the shop looks good" in a very casual way, wanting to make a purchase. This is where dilution affects the wide-slice customers, the most likely people to purchase any unfamiliar game (and, I aver, especially indie games).

      Does that make any sense?

      CU

      Posted By: Malcolm Sheppardb) Anybody who's had a chance to analyze OBS sales knows that people do in fact buy things after category-directed browsing and that vendor-indexed reviews don't matter much (and of course, the theoretical guy who gets into gaming via web browsing is not even the subject of this thread). An RPGNet review sometimes matters, but not much. Browsing is a huge source of direct sales. The larger the small press index, the more difficult browsing is, the less there is to go around and the more people inadvertently pick up crap. The metaphor of a store with shelves is pretty off, since these issues take place outside of and before actual brick and mortal get into it, and across multiple consumer and retail sales.

      Of course, there are times when shelf space does come into it. Take the Forge Booth at Gen Con 2006, which posted record sales -- but awful sales past the top tier. Dilution has already had an effect in the indie-brand corner.
      \nAnd I see this only AFTER my long 2 part post.

      From what I've heard, what Malcolm here is saying about OBS is right -- while overall sales are up across the sites, individual publishers' sales (anecdotally) seem to be down.

      CU
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nMalcolm Sheppard \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2007 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I'm also referring to the front page bump effect -- people buy the stuff they see and I've noticed that's a more powerful indicator of where my sales will go than anything anyone's saying. Budo does pretty well despite the resentment that hardcore Wushu people have expressed because I didn't somehow get their permission and violated some special Wushu design principles or something. The reviews have been so so. I think it's a great game, but I have to admit that a lot of its success is an indexing effect. The listed system only has three publishers (Wushu creator Dan Bayn, Clint Krause and me) and it's cross-listed with a genre that has few small press releases. "Asian" is also the first Fantasy subcategory release.

      The point is that even though I put some thought into it (and even revised it, because the set of people who understand koryu bugei terms is smaller than I thought:-), it has multiple success factors that have nothing to do with its content except for filtering tags.

      Now consider, by comparison, that the thing *outranking* it in the Fantasy/Asian category right now is a pdf of The Book of Five Rings: a text with multiple free translations online. I'm not saying it sucks, but it's not exactly being driven by community-creating hype. It's taking advantage of those same indexing properties.

      I think better segmenting by quality and publisher scale would be great. One of the reasons IPR was such a good fit for Aeternal Legends is because the products are reviewed and identified with a particular segment. When RPGNow started the Edge thing pre-merger I welcomed it for much the same reason. But I think that in addition, giving a little thought to what *should* be entering such systems and holding back until you can make it really worth the consumer's while would be a great idea.

      \n Hi Chad,

      I think it's really important to consider this all in the context of the dilution argument made in this thread- that small publishers are glutting and choking out the "industry" publishers (I use quotes here not to dismiss the term, but to note that we're talking about a very narrow definition, which counts folks like R.Talsorian and Fiery Dragon but not other small publishers in a pretty arbitrary fashion, so it's important to keep in mind when I use the word).

      As many have noted, the D20 glut does fill the shelves and weigh down the FLGS economy- but how many people note the important aspect that D20 material is supplemental to WOTC's D20 games, and not alternative competing games? It's weird to point to, say, Capes and say "Fie unto you for crowding out the shelves!" when in fact, it's folks who are basically working to make supplemental material FOR the industry (or at least the leader of the industry). And, if we're talking about the casual wide slice customer who is looking for variety, I have a hard time believing that the indie wave is working against their needs as much as the outsourced voluntary supplement treadmill that is SRD/D20/OGL.

      Second, concerning online sites, it's also ridiculous to point to the small publishers as the cause of what effectively is bad site design and store management. In retail, the smart owner puts the prettiest and most consistent sellers up front and the nichey-kitschy stuff in the back. It's like pointing to the publishers of books as the problem if you walk into a messy, kluttered, mismanaged bookstore. Amazon is a simple example of a site that deals with massive inventory intelligently.

      In the long run, we're going to see more online stores "get it" and do just that- put the biggest/most consistent sellers up front, and possibly even get better at simply shuffling dead inventory to ebay or half.com as soon as it becomes apparent it has no real value for their business.

      I'm in agreeance that it's a tiny group who are focused on one set of publishers to the exclusion of others, but if we aren't talking about them- has the purchasing ratios of the wide slice customer changed -that- much? Haven't they been buying random small press stuff from the beginning, whether we're talking Timelords or Usagi Yojimbo? And I find it hard to imagine that small press rpgs have had a bigger impact on the wide slice gamer's dollar than CCGs, collectible mini's, the euroboardgame revival and the exploding market of videogames over the last 10 years.

      And if the purchasing ratio has changed that much AND wide slice customers make up that much of the market, do we point to small press publishers for meeting the demand for variety or to the industry publishers for failing to do so? You could easily say that the glut of publishing is on the hands of the industry publishers for pouring out lots of investment and supplements for a few game lines instead of diversifying in the first place.

      I feel like the initial argument is like pointing to 10 guys who get overpaid on their Social Security checks and blaming them for the collapse of the nation's infrastructure- it requires ignoring larger context to an amazing degree. I mean, the argument has been made before that general mismanagement, bad business decisions, and a poor distribution system would lead to bad times, and that instead of examining those very real issues, instead pointing to publishers who are choosing to either not to take that road or interact with it in a very limited fashion, as the source of woe is the structure of the argument.

      (last 6 words edited in to complete the thought, with late night posting and all)

      Posted By: bankueiHi Chad,

      I think it's really important to consider this all in the context of the dilution argument made in this thread- that small publishers are glutting and choking out the "industry" publishers (I use quotes here not to dismiss the term, but to note that we're talking about a very narrow definition, which counts folks like R.Talsorian and Fiery Dragon but not other small publishers in a pretty arbitrary fashion, so it's important to keep in mind when I use the word).
      \nThat is not in fact the argument. The argument is that small publishers with half-baked products produced on what an accountant would term a loss are choking out other small publishers. Larger publishers have more distinct sales and distribution channels.\n

      As many have noted, the D20 glut does fill the shelves and weigh down the FLGS economy- but how many people note the important aspect that D20 material is supplemental to WOTC's D20 games, and not alternative competing games? It's weird to point to, say, Capes and say "Fie unto you for crowding out the shelves!" when in fact, it's folks who are basically working to make supplemental material FOR the industry (or at least the leader of the industry). And, if we're talking about the casual wide slice customer who is looking for variety, I have a hard time believing that the indie wave is working against their needs as much as the outsourced voluntary supplement treadmill that is SRD/D20/OGL.
      \nThere's not really much difference between the supplement treadmill and games produced in the bottom tier indie treadmill. They both serve to fill out space that exists at the sufferance of games in their segment that are more popular by an order of magnitude and manage to suffocate each other. See Keith Senkowski at:

      http://bob-goat.livejournal.com/91997.html\n

      Second, concerning online sites, it's also ridiculous to point to the small publishers as the cause of what effectively is bad site design and store management. In retail, the smart owner puts the prettiest and most consistent sellers up front and the nichey-kitschy stuff in the back. It's like pointing to the publishers of books as the problem if you walk into a messy, kluttered, mismanaged bookstore. Amazon is a simple example of a site that deals with massive inventory intelligently.
      \nExcept that it doesn't. Amazon thrives on the externalities of products that are already successful. Its attempts to do the same thing as OBS have generally been awful, unless you enjoy buying Star Wars fanfic. Lulu's not much better, despite its powerful tools self-organizing tools.\n

      I'm in agreeance that it's a tiny group who are focused on one set of publishers to the exclusion of others, but if we aren't talking about them- has the purchasing ratios of the wide slice customer changed -that- much? Haven't they been buying random small press stuff from the beginning, whether we're talking Timelords or Usagi Yojimbo? And I find it hard to imagine that small press rpgs have had a bigger impact on the wide slice gamer's dollar than CCGs, collectible mini's, the euroboardgame revival and the exploding market of videogames over the last 10 years.
      \nIt has nothing to do with purchasing ratios. It has to do with the degree to which a browsing customer buying small press is basically gambling instead of making a confident purchase, based on how crowded it all is.
      \n

      \n

      \n\nMalcolm Sheppard \n\n\n

      \n\nDecember 2007 \n

      \n

      And if the purchasing ratio has changed that much AND wide slice customers make up that much of the market, do we point to small press publishers for meeting the demand for variety or to the industry publishers for failing to do so? You could easily say that the glut of publishing is on the hands of the industry publishers for pouring out lots of investment and supplements for a few game lines instead of diversifying in the first place.
      \nLarge publishers are literally not in the same category. By "literally," I mean that there are top level indices devoted to them and no point making the comparison. I am not talking about theory, but what happens when you hit RPGNow. There are 15 companies that are direct-linked out of a combination of popularity and courtesy, and everybody else. Those 15 aren't crowding out everybody else nor (and this is where I disagree with Gareth slightly) vice versa. Hell, even some of those 15 are dead wood. (DP9? Seriously?)

      IPR is a better bet because its products have been vetted. The Forge community has introduced a similar process by having members with cred tell other people that their games suck, are tantamount to stealing from the customer and should be in a special, different booth.\n
      I feel like the initial argument is like pointing to 10 guys who get overpaid on their Social Security checks and blaming them for the collapse of the nation's infrastructure- it requires ignoring larger context to an amazing degree.
      \nI think it would really help you make your point if you went with less metaphor and more fact. When I talk about how and when things sell I'm talking about *real* things. It is not my pet theory that front page exposure causes significant sales bumps. It's what actually happens when something of mine hits the front page. When I talk about the treadmill effect being universal and not the result of the system you think is Bad and Wrong, I'm not pulling it out of my ass -- I'm referring to people who identified themselves as having been shuffled off onto it in *your* community.

      \n Really, I think the best way to organize indie writing is the standard B&N / Amazon model: allow authors a certain number of tags they can assign to books.

      Amber, for example:

      Tabletop RPGS > Diceless
      Tabletop RPGS > Fantasy > Licensed World

      Allowing folks to search by mechanics and tone would make it easier for people to find summaries of works relevant to their interests. When I want a cyberpunk short story book written by new authors, I can dump all of that into Amazon and find exactly what I'm looking for. With a smaller, more hand-carried selection, I think the system would work even better; heck, most of the time you could contact an author in this scene yourself if you were so inclined. It's just a matter of finding that book that matches how you think or feel at the time.

      Posted By: bankueiI think it's really important to consider this all in the context of the dilution argument made in this thread- that small publishers are glutting and choking out the "industry" publishers (I use quotes here not to dismiss the term, but to note that we're talking about a very narrow definition, which counts folks like R.Talsorian and Fiery Dragon but not other small publishers in a pretty arbitrary fashion, so it's important to keep in mind when I use the word).
      \nI don't think that's the argument at all.

      I am continually seeing a distinction being drawn in this thread between "small publishers" and "industry publishers" when we everybody we're talking about is a small publisher. That's not the divide. The divide is between people who are publishing-to-publish *into the market* -- at a loss -- and people who are publishing-for-profit into the market. (Note that I don't count those people who publish-to-publish on the web or blog or fora in the former category; creation for creation's sake is fantastic. But if you're going to play in business, the whole system only works if people are playing by the same rules.)

      I think the argument is that publishers-operating-at-a-loss are choking not just publishers-operating-as-a-business but also other publishers-operating-at-a-loss, with side issues about quality levels of products and customer overload/confusion.\n
      Posted By: bankuei It's weird to point to, say, Capes and say "Fie unto you for crowding out the shelves!" when in fact, it's folks who are basically working to make supplemental material FOR the industry (or at least the leader of the industry).
      \nWell, I never said that about Capes. Indeed, I was the one who started talking about d20 crowding.\n
      Posted By: bankueiAnd, if we're talking about the casual wide slice customer who is looking for variety, I have a hard time believing that the indie wave is working against their needs as much as the outsourced voluntary supplement treadmill that is SRD/D20/OGL.
      \nBoth are true. The casual wide-slice customer is affected by glut, be it d20 glut, indie glut, or (as I think, both). But indie glut most affects the wide-slice customer who's already interested in indie games.\n
      Posted By: bankueiSecond, concerning online sites, it's also ridiculous to point to the small publishers as the cause of what effectively is bad site design and store management.(snip) It's like pointing to the publishers of books as the problem if you walk into a messy, kluttered, mismanaged bookstore.
      \nThat's not a bad point, but consider that the small size and insular nature of the gaming industry means that gaming publishers have a disproportionate voice with the major sales online sales sites compared to a B&M bookstore.\n
      Posted By: bankueiIn the long run, we're going to see more online stores "get it" and do just that- put the biggest/most consistent sellers up front,
      \nNot necessarily: the OBS storefronts have gone to weird algorithms to determine what's a "hot-seller." For example, pre-merger, T&J used to be in the top 10 or 20 non-d20 RPGs in the topsellers list. Now, it's near the bottom of the top 100. The online storefronts are not tracking biggest/most consistent sellers any more.\n
      Posted By: bankueiI'm in agreeance that it's a tiny group who are focused on one set of publishers to the exclusion of others, but if we aren't talking about them- has the purchasing ratios of the wide slice customer changed -that- much? Haven't they been buying random small press stuff from the beginning, whether we're talking Timelords or Usagi Yojimbo?
      \nSomething has changed. The merger of the two biggest online RPG shops (RPGNow and DTRPG) has led to higher sales overall, but nearly every publisher I've talked to has reported lower individual sales. The merger combined two gigantic dBs of product, creating a glut.\n
      Posted By: bankueiAnd if the purchasing ratio has changed that much AND wide slice customers make up that much of the market, do we point to small press publishers for meeting the demand for variety or to the industry publishers for failing to do so? You could easily say that the glut of publishing is on the hands of the industry publishers for pouring out lots of investment and supplements for a few game lines instead of diversifying in the first place.
      \nTo reiterate: we're not talking (at least I'm not talking) about glut affecting D&D/WotC. I am talking about glut affecting me, Fred Hicks, Ron Edwards, Clinton Nixon, Jared Soresenson, John Wick, Tony LB, Clash Bowley, Malcom Sheppard, etc. etc.

      That is, your indie friends and neighbors. Not some faceless super-corp.

      \n Hi Chad,

      I'm also with you that everyone is a small publisher, which again, is why "industry" as defined by the argument put forth is pretty poorly defined.

      I think the argument is that publishers-operating-at-a-loss are choking not just publishers-operating-as-a-business but also other publishers-operating-at-a-loss, with side issues about quality levels of products and customer overload/confusion.

      I don't think that's the point of the argument at all, because the 10,000 mini-supplement PDFs which typically flood your online retailer are made to get profit- they are publishers operating as a business, even if that business plan is very loose and profit is not the core objective.

      I'm still not seeing any evidence that more small games is hurting everyone more than the fact we have a hobby that has lots of new, competing hobbies as well as a historically poor recruitment of new player base.

      As far as I can tell, the only evidence is simply a few mismanaged online sites, and the reason I point to "in the long run" sorts of improvements, is that like all other business, the incentive is there to make more profit. Whether this or that specific outlet does it now, or in the next year, is not so relevant as the fact that at some point, in the future, as long as we have an internet and internet based sales, that someone will eventually pick up on doing the more sensible thing, at some point.

      \n Wow. I go away for a couple of days to help my folks lug some furniture around and come back to a three page thread. Anyways, I'm going to reply to a bunch of posts all at once, so hope this doesn't get too confusing. Hm. Looks like I need to break this up into two posts. lol.
      \n
      \nEarlier it was suggested that no one in the industry has a clue about the industry. That is patently untrue. There are a good number of folks that own companies in this industry that understand it very well and has spent years or decades gathering information on it. Its only that this data is all internalized and gathered by each firm independently. Some of my long time friends in the industry are always collecting and updating this stuff and when we get the opportunity to, we share and compare to make sure we all have the most up to date info as possible. To persons who haven't been operating within the industry for long, or at all, it only seems like there is no data. This is because the industry is just too small for anyone to make a profit collecting, publishing or selling it as a business. And most companies are privately held, so an enterprising outside party can't just scour SEC filing to garner it.
      \n
      \nI doubt I have done a fraction of the consulting that Gareth has, but I have done some in the past. In the future, we are considering hosting publishing seminars at our retail locations. As for sharing what I know, I am always willing to answer questions online. Otherwise, I can tell you that Chris Clark from Inner City Game Designs, Bill Corrie from HinterWelt Enterprises and myself have begun a collaboration on the Game Publishers' Digest, which will be released as 46 topical issues in PDF format, then later published as a four book set. Marcus King of Titan Games has also agreed to write commentary on each subject from a retailer's perspective. But the project has just begun and we want to put the first 5-10 inssues in the can before releasing the first one.
      \n
      \n>>You mentioned earlier that there is a difference between a game designer and a game publisher. What is the difference and how do some game designers muck shit up and make mistakes?

      \n Hey Chad,\n
      Posted By: chaduI think that it's more gamesin general(big or small) that causes general glut; more small games may onlyspecificallyhurt other small games by causing glut in the small games market.
      \nSo, you're making a different point than Gareth?       \n\nchadu \n\n\n

      \n\nDecember 2007 \n

      \n
      \n
      Posted By: Paul CzegePosted By: chaduI think that it's more gamesin general(big or small) that causes general glut; more small games may onlyspecificallyhurt other small games by causing glut in the small games market.
      \nSo, you're making a different point than Gareth? A thriving online community designing and releasing nicely done free pdf roleplaying games is just as much a glut problem from your perspective as a glut of games the designers are charging for? You're not saying "don't publish if you don't want to be a business," you're saying "don't design"?

      I'm saying "don't publish and operate in the market with a for-pay product if you don't want to be a business." That's my opinion.

      I hold that more products equals glut, whether they're utter crap or utter genius. "Glut" is a value neutral term, meaning "increased volume." (As I've noted earlier, the glut/increased product volume issue is simply a mathematical discussion, and completely unrelated to relative quality of products. A glut of awesome work is still a glut, and will impact across-the-board sales in the marketplace.)

      If you want to design, go for it. If you want to offer your work for free, all the better.

      But if you want to offer your work on the market, charging customers for the product, it's distasteful -- and harmful to the market -- to both undercharge for the product and to "spam" the marketplace with undervalued products.

      The former leads to an undervaluing of the work necessary to create a game (by authors, artists, editors, and layout folks), and the latter leads to glut/increased product volume.

      To sum up, my position is that if you want to play in the for-pay market, play by the for-pay rules. If you just want to design games and have people play them, release them for free (and bless you!).

      Is that clearer?


      CU

      Posted By: bankueiHi Chad,

      From what you've just said, it seems to me that the real issue to look at is:

      a) the wider context of the games market (videogames, CCGs, MMO's etc.)
      \nI remain unconvinced that videogames, CCGs, MMORPGs, etc., have sucked away wide-slice customers. I am willing to agree that narrow-slice (possibly in the classic D&D/hack 'n slash slice) customers have been sucked away by such.

      Interestingly, I know several people who wouldn't be caught dead playing an tabletop RPG, but love CRPGs to pieces. I think there's a customer sector there that's not fully understood.
      \n
      Posted By: bankueib) poor distribution (both online and retail)

      as major factors in industry downturn and "overproduction" of small publishers as a non-relevant issue.
      \nI will agree that poor distribution is the key issue, but once again reiterate that in such a narrow (and in some cases, almost singular) channel, publisher overproduction (be it small or large publishers) can overwhelm that channel.

      If distribution was more diverse, publisher overproduction would have less of an impact. However, In a channelized distribution set-up (and possibly only in a channelized set-up), overproduction has a disproportionate impact on the market.

      To re-emphasize: the lion's share of the blame can be directly left at the feet of the distro channel (whatever it is), but overproduction of products (especially those created at a loss by publishers) swamps that narrow channel, and publishers-at-a-loss are thus somewhat culpable.

      (I think analogies can be drawn with the current comic book distro market here, with the collapse to effective a single distributor, but I am not fully educated on the comics distro market, so I leave it to more informed heads to discuss.)

      \nI am curious why you repeatedly and continually are seeming to insist in your replies that I am focusing solely on the effect of small press product volume and sales on the entire gaming market (video games, board games, card games, etc.).

      While I believe there is a ripple-effect through directly related markets (small press RPGs and three-tier RPGS), and the ripple weakens dramatically as you cross categories (to boardgames, cardgames, videogames, etc.), that's secondary.

      Let me start from zero: A high volume of small press tabletop RPG products primarily affects the small press tabletop RPG market.

      Or, to rephrase:
      * A high volume of videogame products primarily affects the videogame market.
      * A high volume of cars primarily affects the automobile market.
      * A high volume of apples primarily affects the apple-selling market.

      Do you agree or disagree with this basic assertion: "The number of [THINGS] available for sale affects the buying and selling of [THINGS]?"

      If you disagree, I may as well stop talking.

      Posted By: Paul CzegeYeah, I'm not seeing how it hangs together. How is designing and offering my work for free as a nicely done pdf not spamming the marketplace with an "undervalued product"?
      \nBecause "free" equals "not in the market." A free product doesn't compete for dollars.

      That being said, given the nature of the online channels we have currently, placing a free product on a sales site very well could be "spamming the market" or "contributing to glut," simply in increasing the amount of material a customer has to look through. That is, while a free product doesn't compete for dollars, it does compete for eyes/attention.

      Glut = increased volume and market = money are two separate -- if entwined -- issues. The entanglement comes, I think, especially online from the limited number of sales/distribution channels (and their operation and procedures.)

      Here's an illustrative example involving apples.

      Glut = the apple stand is four crates wide.

      If there are four crates of apples, one can easily tell that one is Macintosh, the next is Gala, the next is Granny Smith, and the next is Jonagold.

      If suddenly the stand gets twelve more crates of different apples, those sixteen crates can only be stacked in four ranks of four. The front rank will obscure the signs -- and even the crates themselves -- of the dozen behind. A casual customer may assume that there are only four types of apple available at the moment. Another customer may peer into the depths, see three more rows of crates with different apples, and throw up his hands and walk away. However, the customer who already bought some Honeycrisps from that apple stand (or has come specifically to look for Honeycrisps because he's heard this stand has them) is much less daunted by the four ranks of four.

      Market = most apples cost around a quarter (plus or minus).
      Placing eight more crates of free apples next to the quarter-priced ones makes it even harder to even see which apples are for sale. Remember, the stand is only four crates wide, and we just added two more rows of crates. It's even more difficult to see what's for sale in the back now. However, the free apples aren't directly competing for the customer's dollar -- they're more likely to indirectly affect sales (the customer purchases fewer of the for-pay apples and makes up the slack with the free ones).

      Underpriced apples -- say, a dime (because the orchard owner makes his grandkids pick the apples for free rather than paying them) -- can cause the same visual confusion as free products, but then there are other issues involved. If an apple producer decides that they have no need to make any money on selling their apples, and wish only to get them out there and undervalue the apples' price rather than offer them for free, they are causing customers to look at the quarter cost apples and the dime cost apples and compare prices. This can lead to the customer thinking that the quarter cost apples are overpriced, despite being priced reasonably -- it's the dime cost apples that are underpriced. Thus, the dime apples are directly competing with the quarter apples for both dollars and attention.\n
      Posted By: Paul CzegeYou don't believe that a thriving online community designing and releasing nicely done free pdf games is just as much a problem for the market as it would be if the designers were charging for the games?
      \nNo and yes.

      If the free games are not in the marketplace/sales site/game shop, then no -- I don't think they compete directly, and are not much of a problem.

      They may, however, affect the market as a whole indirectly, because if lots of well-done games are free and readily available, the number of customers even entering a shop willing to spend dollars on games may reduce somewhat. (However, I think this is a vastly lower probability problem. The existence of RISUS or TSOY being freely available isn't costing, say, DitV any sales.)

      There's a benefit to games being creative art products here. You could set up a for-pay gameshop and a free-game shop across the street from each other, and I don't think the free-game shop would necessarily mess up the for-pay one, unlike if you set up a free-apple stand across the street from a for-pay apple stand.

      Posted By: guildofblades
      If an artist can't finish a B&W of modest complexity in an hour's time, then odds are pretty good they aren't half as skilled a professional as they would like to believe.
      \nAbsolutely true, so far as it goes.
      And I fully support the right of a publisher to offer what they can realistically afford (it's far better than offering more and never paying, as some companies seem inclined to do), so don't think I'm beating you up over your rates. I've definitely seen worse, and as long as the check comes through you're one of the good guys, in my book.

      However....

      Even though any artist can likely turn out a drawing (of some sort) in an hour, alotting only an hour for a drawing means (regardless of how relatively good the artist is) that you are getting a drawing which is not all it could be. Paying even slightly more would allow the same artist to take more time to refine and improve the drawing while still making the same hourly rate. That will generally result in a better drawing from the same artist.

      Pay me for an hour, and I could knock out a modest drawing for you, no problem. But pay me enough that I could put two hours into it, and you'd get three times the drawing. So it's up to you to decide which drawing you would want based on finding a balance between what your budget will allow and what level of quality you feel is "good enough" for your needs. If that's a one-hour drawing, so be it, but you should be aware of the choice you're making. After all, even Elmore isn't going to give you his best work if he feels rushed.

      I also believe (even though some artists are certainly faster than others in achieving a comparable level of quality) that a good many of the artists who demand higher rates don't do so because they are so wildly talented they can turn out their best work in an hour and just want more money for their time. I'd bet most do it because they prefer not to rush out work that is not up to their personal standards.

      Just some food for thought.

      \n Hey Chad,

      Y'know, I think the "yes and no" and "maybe" and "dollars" vs "eyes/attention" qualifiers are uncertainties resulting from perspective.

      If what we're doing is an industry and dilution is a problem, then it's a Tragedy of the Commons that's simply not solvable by trying to convince market participants not to participate (or that they actually don't want to participate). It's in the individual interest of participants to exploit the shared resource (customer attention and dollars) to the best of their ability. You solve a Tragedy of the Commons via regulation, or force (neither of which is going to happen here).

      But I don't think it is an industry. I think it's a hobby. And that it's not a Tragedy of the Commons. I know that I've personally put more money into Peter Adkison's pocket, and into the pockets of other hobby participants in the past five years than in the entire previous 24 years I've been involved with it. Participants in a hobby grow it, in real terms (money spent, and time and energy invested). If the merger of RPGNow and DTRPG is resulting in more stuff for sale and higher overall sales, that's because we're seeing an expansion of participation in the core roleplaying hobby activity: making and expressing a sense of the world, the human experience, and the self via shared experiences of imagination and story making.

      And structuring the experiences of others is a part of feeding what you've learned back into that core activity. Design itself is fundamental to the core activity. True participation entails being both a producer and a consumer.

      I expect the future holds continued expansion of participation, by folks with a passion for both the experience and the design facets of the core activity, and at a pace that's increasingly going to take your breath away.

      This only produces anxiety when you're looking at it from a market perspective. A decline in revenue feels like it's squeezing you out of what you've chosen to do. But that's not true at all. When you transition from a market perspective to a core activity perspective you're struck by just how much more there is you can do. And the folks that do so aren't anxious at all. The guy who comes to Gen Con with a hundred copies of his game and a goal of selling them all, but who otherwise doesn't participate in the design community, comes home disappointed. The folks who play and playtest the games of other designers, and learn from them, who provide feedback to games in development, their sales are evergreen. The hobby knows its core activity.

      Paul

      Posted By: guildofblades>>

      Oh, I totally understand and totally agree. And that is totally their choice. However, an artist making this choice really shouldn't be trying to stand on some kind of moral high ground and complaining that all those companies that don't want to pay them those rates are out there "ripping artists off and taking advantage of them". That's the rub right there.
      \nAgreed.
      I've never accused a company that actually pays what they promise of "ripping artists off and taking advantage of them". As long as they are paid, the rates just are what they are. I'd certainly like it if the industry standard rates were higher, but hey....\n
      I as a business have no "moral" obligation to pay some artists or all artists $100 a page for B&W just because that is what some artist somewhere feel that is what they are worth. That's like some guy looking to work the assembly line in a GM plant coming in and saying, I know you guys pay your union like workers like $29 an hour, but I am special and unique so you have a moral obligation to both give me this job and pay me $50 an hour instead. Publishers expect a good effort, no matter how little they're paying) . Artists in the rpg industry, generally speaking, aren't getting union wages (working elsewhere pays more by several orders of magnitude, there's where you'll find the equivalent of "union wages") because they're usually not actually knocking out a rush job in an hour. They want to do a good job too, nobody wants to be seen as a hack who knocks out half-assed work. So, in comparison, they're usually getting something closer to an illegal immigrant migrant worker's pay, and griping because they'd like to make minimum wage.

      Posted By: Jarrod20 bucks a page. 1 Page an hour. 20 bucks an hour to draw orcs. Sounds good to me.
      \nIt's great.

      If you're fast. Or don't care whether the work you're putting out there for everyone to see is your best.

      Don't get me wrong, there are drawings I could do well in an hour. There are drawings I could do well in twenty minutes. But many of the drawings I've had people commission are not the sort that I'd be comfortable rushing out in two hours, much less an hour.

      Though really, twenty bucks for a drawing with modest detail is not a bad rate for this industry. I've run into people who expect that for five.


      Edit:
      Thinking about all this, it occurs to me that there may be some confusion about what goes into a drawing for commission. Folks may be seeing "an hour to make a drawing" and wondering what the big deal is. Well, the "for commission" is important to consider. When doing a drawing for myself, I can just draw it. I make all the decisions, and it can go pretty fast. With a commission, that's a whole other ballgame.

      I may have to dig up references, if there's something specific the publisher is looking for. Or maybe I'll get lucky and be able to go straight to work, but I can't count on it. Then I've got to do a sketch and send it off for review. There will probably be revisions, maybe even multiple rounds of review/revision (I've spent more than an hour just in scanning and emailing and revising to meet what the publisher has in his head - if the publisher isn't good at explaining what he's looking for, a one hour drawing could eat up most of your day). And then, after the sketch phase, and the revision phase is all over, then I can draw it. Then I can hope the publisher doesn't want more revisions, or a redraw. All of that has to be factored into that "one hour drawing"
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

    • \n
      \n

      \n\ncharlequin \n\n\n

      Posted By: madelf
      Even though any artist can likely turn out a drawing (of some sort) in an hour, alotting only an hour for a drawing means (regardless of how relatively good the artist is) that you are getting a drawing which is not all it could be.
      \nRegardless of the specific rate being paid, too, an artist isn't really just paying for their labor in assembling the piece with their contracted rate. A contracted artist, like all subcontractors, is paying a variety of expenses (payroll taxes, insurance, etc.) that an employer would partially or fully cover for a staff employee; they have operational expenses (like art materials) to pay for that would be covered by the employer if they were working in a "staff artist" environment; they have to spend hours in maintenance of their business that come away from the time actually used to illustrate.

      Edit: And Madelf added some other important notes just above me.

      Posted By: chaduBecause "free" equals "not in the market." A free product doesn't compete for dollars.
      \nChad,

      I'm going to come at this from a consumer POV for one second, because I don't feel that this is the whole picture.

      Right now I have more games than I am going to play in the next 5 years. I have gotten to the point where I am very specific about what games I pick up, or even download for free, because I have such a glut of material that I already have access to -- not even that I have to purchase or acquire -- that I have no need to get more. In fact, I sometimes feel mildly annoyed when folks want me to review their game, even if they're offering me comps because I don't fucking have time for that. (I usually do it anyway, because I'm a chump.)

      So in this one tiny corner of the market over here you are not competing with other for pay products for my money. You are competing with everything I have available to me for my attention. If I get, say Swashbucklers or not will not depend on if I can afford it or not economically with all the other games I have to buy, but whether I can bring myself to even think about it with all the other games I have to think about.

      Seriously, if I want your game I have enough money that I can pick it and a bunch of other games up at the same time. But to even make me want your game you have to compete not with the other games for my money (who cares, I've got it), but for my time an attention (which is severely limited).

      Also, there is a degree to which it is my attention with other RPGs. I spend a certain amount of time a month doing RPG stuff, and a certain amount, say, watching movies. At this point in my life, I'm pretty stable about how much of which I'm going to do -- so better movies or video games aren't likely to decrease my RPGing time. I have a pretty good feeling this isn't how it works with say, many d20 players drifting to World of Warcraft, but it is how it works in my life. I've got X hours a month that go to RPGing, and those X hours aren't getting sucked out by other hobbies. But in those X hours I do not have time for all of you, even the all of you that are awesome, and so I will not give you my time or attention because there is too much else to do in my allotted time.

      Now what does this mean to the market and the glut and all that? I dunno, and frankly I don't care enough to try to figure it out. I'm not saying anything about that, and I'm not publishing or Publishing RPGs atm. I am just saying that at this point my personal response to glut isn't that folks have to compete for my money, its that they have to compete for my attention, that it is RPG against RPG, and that free or pay makes very little difference save that I may be more likely (being brainwashed) to assume an RPG I have to pay for is actually better done.

      Maybe I'm the exception that proves the rule, but there it is.
      \n

      Posted By: Paul CzegeHey Chad,

      Posted By: chaduI think that it's more gamesin general(big or small) that causes general glut; more small games may onlyspecificallyhurt other small games by causing glut in the small games market.
      \nSo, you're making a different point than Gareth? A thriving online community designing and releasing nicely done free pdf roleplaying games is just as much a glut problem from your perspective as a glut of games the designers are charging for? You're not saying "don't publish if you don't want to be a business," you're saying "don't design"?

      Paul

      I think it's a pity that there isn't nearly as much variety and activity in free game production as there used to be. I think the era of games like Thrash was marvelous. Current free games are basically short sketches produced for contests that may or may not become polished later.

      I also think that in a way, open source has actually poisoned the culture of free game design, because gamers now impose mental speedbumps on their approach to inspiring work instead of just taking things and running with them. While I'm sure some people were frightened by Palladium and TSR, their attempts to control house rules didn't really work. Things like the glorious, uneven content of BJ Zanzibar's WoD site and other grey-area game hacks are gone and there's a pervasive feeling that mere fair use isn't enough to justify getting it out there; you need some tedious supporting legalese or other community permission, to boot.

      (This sort of thing has a bad effect on companies, too. One example is Technomancer Press, which was pilloried in part for exercising fair use. They had the temerity to make D&D compatible products without using a license. Remember when TSR actually had to *fight* the fans to attack people like this? Keep in mind I'm not talking about their claims of compatibility, which were not so smart.)

      I'm not sure, but I suspect that there's a connection between the bloated, uneven state of small for-pay RPG publishers and the simultaneous decline of the interesting, chaotic and fearless free games scene.
      \n

      Posted By: Malcolm SheppardThings like the glorious, uneven content of BJ Zanzibar's WoD site and other grey-area game hacks are gone and there's a pervasive feeling that mere fair use isn't enough to justify getting it out there;
      \nIt is still out there. You just can't see it any more, because the web's too big. It's not centralized any more, but everybody and their brother still throws My Houserules That Totally Rock up on their web page or a message board or their LJ or some such. Nobody links to it much, so it doesn't rate on search engines, and you can't find it unless you know about it offline.

      That, and a lot of people got stars in their eyes and thought they could monetize My Houserules That Totally Rock. But after the crash, and with 4E coming down the pike, you'll see more of it creep out into freeware again.

      This is all really pretty much the same argument that every other IP-centric market is thrashing through now. In the end, it's going to be damn near impossible to make a living from your IP as a private individual, but much, much easier to capture a small income stream from it without involving any "publisher" if you don't want to.

      Posted By: Malcolm SheppardI'm not sure, but I suspect that there's a connection between the bloated, uneven state of small for-pay RPG publishers and the simultaneous decline of the interesting, chaotic and fearless free games scene.
      \nI was just talking to Fred Hicks about this recently, in the context of the original free release of things like Fudge and Fate (a hack of Fudge) and high-quality free stuff like Scarlet Wake. There was also, recently, a bunch of indie designers who had free games on their personal websites: Zak Arntson, Jared Sorenson, Paul Czege, and Vincent Baker, among others. I've played a bunch of those actually: Shadows, Metal Opera, Otherkind, The World The Flesh & The Devil, The Valedictorian's Death, Nicotine Girls. Many of those are still up, so I think there's a good chance that free games will be making a comeback, once the tide swings around again. Scratch under the surface of the indie games community and you find a lot of good, playtested, relatively polished games that are up for free. (There also all of Greg Stolze's games that have been successfully ransomed). Clinton's new online text of TSOY is a cool move in that direction, as is the posting of the free parts of Spirit of the Century / Fate 3. Personally, I would love it if we could convince Jason Morningstar to make some of his wackier projects (like Drowning & Falling, Business Solutions, or Medical Hospital) into free games with, say, charity donation buttons or ransoms, since D&F was a charity project anyway.

      This could be a core part of the community again. It would just take people making it happen.

      Posted By: Brand_RobinsSometimes I used to post about it on my blog. Sometimes at length. Some people thought it was nifty, no one ever actually seemed to use any of it to play though.

      So I still do it, I just don't bother posting it anymore. Too much energy to write it up so people who aren't me or mine will understand it.
      \nI think a lot of hobbyist publishers feel the same way, but instead of giving up on posting it they slap a price tag on it instead. It's like they're saying "Oh yeah, you don't want my free thing? Fine, then you can't have it! You have to pay for it instead." And the thing is, sometimes that works, since there's pervasive mistrust of free stuff, this attitude that if you don't have to pay for something, it's because it's not worth anything.

      And really, if I was a little less hyper-critical of my own work, I could see doing the same thing. Money, even a pitifully small amount of it, is a form of validation. I know if I ever got around to finishing a game I'd get more satisfaction out of selling 12 copies of it over the course of a year than I would from getting a couple of lukewarm "yeah, nice game, I guess" comments from my LiveJournal buddies. Of course, I'd derive even more satisfaction from giving my game away for free and seeing lots of people play it and have fun with it -- or even seeing lots of people hate it and tell me in no uncertain terms what sucked about it.

      \n\nDaztur \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2007 \n edited December 2007
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n A lot of people are saying that the problem with the "glut" is that people are publishing at a loss and that its impossible to compete with people who are publishing at a loss. I'm not seeing how this is the case for most of the "glut." A lot of it seems to be stuff that's thrown together from the stuff that the author was going to use anyway and he said, "hey I already wrote it as my house rules so why not charge people a few bucks for it." The only costs of doing something through Lulu or a pdf download site are layout and art, which disappear if you can do those yourself or get a friend to do them for you (especially if you're willing to accept crap quality, which I'm sure a lot of people are). Of course this is ignoring opportunity costs, but those don't really apply to people to write up games for the fun of it. So complaining about a glut of POD or PDF products is rather pointless since the costs are so low that there's really nothing to stop people from slapping something together out of their house rules and selling that.

      Basically the old filtering mechanism for keeping out the crap worked like this:
      1. Really really bad games won't sell.
      2. If you game doesn't sell you'll lose money.
      3. If you keep on losing money you'll eventually give up.
      4. Really really bad games will go away and leave space for the good ones (or at least new bad games).

      With POD and PDF that doesn't work anymore since no matter how badly your game sells there's no reason to EVER stop selling it since you don't have to pay for print runs anymore and you've already paid for layout and art. Basically keeping your old crappy game on the market costs nothing, so they never go away.

      A good solution is something like Indie Press Revolution, since they vet books.

      Posted By: noclueTo narrow things down further, I am really most interested in hearing from successful small press publishers who publish for a living.
      \nNote that I do not solely publish games for a living: I have a day-job. That being said, my gaming work (freelance writing and game publishing) generates income that I require (in lieu of picking up a second job).\n
      Posted By: noclueAre they seeing a glut in the market and do they think that sales would be up individually if there were just fewer games on the market? Or do they feel that a healthy and diverse hobby with multiple offerings sustains long term sales? Or are there other opinions?
      \nOver the course of this conversation, I have refined my thoughts and positions somewhat.

      I am leaning towards thinking "glut" is for the most part value-neutral and is simply reflective of larger volume of products. Where glut is bad is in the narrow channels for (for-pay) distribution in this hobby industry -- increased volume leads to lower sales/attention for individual products across all participants in that volume.

      That is the nature of the beast, however. If one wishes to operate as a business selling for-pay games, you've got to deal with that.

      However, an increased number of undervalued for-pay products (produced under different axioms; "make a profit" vs. "make no profit" or even "take a loss") increases market volume unfairly. \n
      Posted By: noclueAs a consumer of small press games, I don't feel overwhelmed by the number of product offerings or unable to get the information I need to make a buy decision. So, it doesn't feel terribly glutted. But I'm not a publisher, just a consumer.
      \nI think that the fact that you are discussing games on the internet, and specifically on SG, puts you automatically into a "more informed consumer" category -- especially about small press games.

      (Levels of information about certain sectors of game product seem to differe between internet fora; one can be highly informed about distro products from top-tier companies and still have no info/insight into small games, and vice versa.)

      Posted By: Matt_SnyderHowever, an increased number of undervalued for-pay products (produced under different axioms; "make a profit" vs. "make no profit" or even "take a loss") increases market volume unfairly.
      \nChad, that's where I get completely lost. Unfairly? What does that even mean?

      What Ithinkit means is "I'm trying to make a 'real' go of this business, and you cheapies are mucking it up for me with your 'fake' attempts at it." In other words "I am trying to make a living of this, stop making it so hard for me since your'e only moonlighting recklessly."

      Well, first off, I don't think I've been that insulting.

      "Unfairly" seemed a better word than "unethically" (which I'm not sure is right either).

      Here's the way I see it: if it costs $10 to make Widget A (cost of goods, fair wage for all contributors) that retails for $20, and that's fairly a standard, ethical, fair cost/price, then what do you call someone (or their actions) who makes Widget B that costs $1 (because of slave labor, free materials, undervaluing personal), is directly compared to the first widget, and is retailed for $1? And note that 10 widget Bs can be made for every widget A.

      What about people who make Widget Cs, that cost $1 to make, but they sell for a dime (taking a loss)?

      The core issue is some people are dealing with the market in a business-oriented fashion and some are dealing with it in a non-business-oriented fashion. \n
      Posted By: Matt_SnyderNow, nevermind that I don't agree that's even happening, I just can't fathom a universe in which I owe it to anybody to back off doing something creative so someone else can keep the lights on. I don't owe that to anybody, and neither does any other publisher. Unfair just has nothing to do with it. I can't make any sense of that at all.

      Is there another better means to explain "unfair"?
      \nI see two "levels" of unfairness: unfair production practices (no fair wage) and increase of market volume (because not limited by fair production practices).

      Maybe I'm wrong.

      What I would like is for all publishers to pay their contributors a fair wage, and price their product according to those costs. (And I believe that doing this would reduce the amount of undervalued products, if but by replacing them with properly valued products.) Is that a better way to say this?

      But let me say again that the core issue seems to be that we have a market, with money being spent, and some producers are operating under one set of rules and others are operating under others. And this is what leads to these discussions.

      Posted By: chaduI have something I want to say about industry standard costs, but it's getting all vague and, frankly, your point about not knowing what the other guy is doing/has done is making me reconsider some stuff. Or at least how to phrase it.

      \n Hi Chad,

      That seems like a bit of a poor analogy given the situation, here, though.

      The flood of cheap stuff here isn't being created by exploited workers who are unaware of market prices and unable to get better wages- for the most part, these are willing folks, hobbyists, who are quite happy to:

      a) do it for free
      b) do it for beer money and maybe enough to kick into the next game
      c) do it with less people, more efficiently and get paid fair wages for freelance writing

      People who consent to a level of work, for a level of compensation, in a situation where they can choose other means of getting 3 square meals a day, are not the same as the Nike sweatshop laborer in a "free trade zone" in Mexico.

      It also doesn't make sense to put small press games that are for profit in the same boat as someone trying to make a living wage- if they make $500 instead of $1000 this year, yeah, it happens, but it's not like babies are going unfed because of it. And, if the small press market were to become completely unprofitable, based on the current business philosophy a lot of these folks are utilizing, they'd still be making games, just with less investment - they still wouldn't be at a loss.

      Second, if your business' plan of exposure and continued sales relies upon curiosity sales over building a consistant customer base (whether through marketing, developing working channels, or simply word of mouth and quality), you're in trouble and with good reason- no market supports that in the long run, just in the same way craps doesn't make for a good source of annual income.

      What I'm not getting here, is what part of this is "unfair"? If folks who have their living wages staked on this business cannot figure out a way to compete against folks who have less experience, less resources, less name recognition, and possibly no business plan at all, either the whole industry is a crap shoot and it's insane to try to pull living wages from it or there is bad business planning on an epic scale.

      What we do see with other areas that have also been blown open by hobbyist markets- like Youtube/Myspace to the music industry or flashgames to the videogame industry, is that there becomes a stratification based on quality, with some crossover, but not to the point that any one is driven completely out by the other.

      I don't know, this line of thought seems more "sour grapes" to me than anything else. Some new technology arrived, and people found alternate means of publishing and channels of distribution that were geometrically more efficient, and also applied business plans ("this is a hobby, not rent") that are keeping them in business. Is it unfair? I don't see why- now all the old players can take the knowledge that does cross over- design, layout, marketing, etc. and they have a lot of examples of people utilizing these things in better or worse ways as datapoints to pull from and apply themselves.

      Instead of asking who deliberately operates at a loss in this game, maybe we need to ask who exactly is operating at a level to consistently earn living wages, and what, exactly, makes that possible.

      (My wild guess is that it's companies who make more money selling novels rather than games. I'd be interested to see what the 2nd tier publishers are doing as well though.)
      \n

      \n\nChristian_Griffen \n\n\n

      Posted By: chaduHere's the way I see it: if it costs $10 to make Widget A (cost of goods, fair wage for all contributors) that retails for $20, and that's fairly a standard, ethical, fair cost/price, then what do you call someone (or their actions) who makes Widget B that costs $1 (because of slave labor, free materials, undervaluing personal), is directly compared to the first widget, and is retailed for $1? And note that 10 widget Bs can be made for every widget A.
      \nChad, I really respect you as a person and as a game designer. So I'm looking at this from the most positive light: you comparing us indie game producers to people who use slave labor in poverty-stricken countries must be just a bad analogy and not really meant in seriousness.

      People who are used as slave labor, including wage slavery, have no choice about their work. It's do or die. I've studied slavery in the modern world somewhat; it's the result of a complex system in which poverty is so epidemic in some countries that people cannot escape slave labor or wage slavery (being kept in perpetual debt by their employer). Now, I've got an M.A. in Political Science and I'm very invested in social justice, so if you've got a forum where you'd like to talk about slavery and abuse of poverty in the world, I'm there.

      People in the United States, including artists, are not in that position. They are no-fucking-where near it. They have choices. In the first place, they choose to pursue art instead of a different career. If they wanted to, they could make a living wage doing 100 other kinds of things. This country is filthy rich. YOU are filthy rich, compared with most of the rest of the world.

      So: indie publishers do not enslave artists. They provide additional opportunities to the market of artists out there in this rich country. No one is forced to take it. No one's livelihood depends on it.

      So let's cut the crap about all this "poor underpaid artists and slave laborers" bullshit and talk about the fact that cheap and free products make it harder for YOU to make the money you want to be making from your game publishing endeavors. If we can be honest about that, we can have a productive conversation. In fact, Ryan is trying to do just that, and his business wisdom shows through in each of his posts. You need to account for the market, for hobbyists doing their thing for cheap, and you need to adjust your business plan accordingly to get what you want out of it.

      Nobody has any sort of right, based on fairness or ethics, to keep other people from producing and selling items at whatever price they decide to do it. To say otherwise in the absence of actually unethical practices (see REAL slave labor, above), is nonsense.

      ETA: sorry about the dogpile, that was a crossposting issue.

      \n EDIT: Yeah, what Christian said (xenopulse). Cross posted with lots of people, it appears!

      Chad, I think you're misreading me as accusing you of being insulting. That wasn't my aim. It's just how I type. I swear and act like a smart butt, and it's more about me sounding goofy than it is about me pissed off about yoU (I ain't, for the record). So, I was earnestly asking that stuff, not accusing. I see how you saw otherwise. No biggie.

      Anyway. First, you gave an answer that unfair was more about selling stuff that should be $20 at something closer to $1. And, ok. But, I don't know anyone who's doing anything near that level of underpricing. KEEP IN MIND! One book shelf and RPGnow and all those guys? TOTALLY off my radar, personally. I sorta get the vibe that's where this crazy stuff happens, maybe?

      So, sure, maybe some stuff like this happens there. I have very little idea of what goes on there. I'm on RPGnow as a publisher, and I hate it. I can't update a damn thing. The service I've received as a publisher is not just lousy, it's non-existent. I've sent emails that were plainly ignored, and I gave up. (Maybe they got spam-boxed or something; I dunno. I still gave up. They're still selling outdated versions of my games, and cutting checks that I can't cash. Thanks for nuttin'!)

      MORE IMPORTANTLY! This thread is ugly. One of the ugliest issues, I think, is this: You and others have taken the position that paying, say, artists an "unfair" wage is unethical to the point of condemning it. Not, well, "I took some staplers from work," but "You're not paying someone who needs to eat, and shame on you!"

      This has three problems. First, we have no idea who the heck you're talking about, if anyone. Instead, it's sort-of, kind-of described as people who "are just game designers" or people who "don't do things in business-fashion." Well, that describes a heck of a lot of people here who publish stuff. So, it's an awfully broad stroke to brush. But, it remains pretty ill-defined.

      I don't see my colleagues (say, pals of mine on IPR) as breaching these ethical boundaries, assuming those boundaries should even exist. I don't see collegaues paying a lot of contributors, for one, nor do I see them treating those contributors unfairly for another. And, I see these colleagues operating pretty ingeniously, albeit at a tidy, contained scope as a "business."

      EDIT: FOrgot the second problem! The second problem is that there is no "fair" standard. Some have declared payments unfair. But, they aren't the arbiter of fair. They're aggrieved. One publisher's fair wage is another one's crime to humanity. There is no arbiter of fair. Which itself seems pretty unfair to me! It just leads to arguing.

      EDIT: Forgot the third problem, which I've said previously. I don't owe a contributor a living. I just don't. They can take it or leave it. If they leave it, fine. I'll try to find someone else who is willing to do the creation for what *I* believe is fair, not what they require to pay rent.

      So, I think you can see that I see a lot of "unfair!" being leveled like shot-gun blast, and it's muddying up the conversation like crazy. How bout some unfair laser beams instead?
