      \n Chad, I think you are being too categorical, yes.

      It's very easy, and flippant, to say "Well, someone actually typed on a forum that it's JUST their hobby, so they're not really serious about business."

      Well, that's a logical fallacy, but probably an understandable one. Nothing about someone saying "I view it as a hobby" means "It's just my hobby, and therefore I under-pay people, and wad up receipts and throw them under my bed and plug my ears and go la-la-la-la when someone even SAYS the word marketing!"

      Indeed, for most of the folks I know, they're "Just hobbies" and "business-fashion" all at the same time. Heck, I think you know them too.

      "I view it as a hobby" could mean any number of things, including "that will one day become my day job" or "that makes a remarkable profit" or "that I track very carefully and mainatin good marketing strategies for" and so on.

      Seeing "hobby" as "not a business plan" is not reading very charitbly, I think. Possibly also getting defensive about one's identity as a "serious businessman" too.

      \n So for me, it comes down to this.

      When I do publishing stuff I'm going to treat myself and all those I'm working with professionally and ethically. That means paying them fairly, treating them honestly and with dignity, and always being clear and above board about the process.

      I will always make a product of which I can be proud. While not everything I've ever written freelance has been my most golden material, I will not publish crap. When it is my own stuff, I'm going to require one step higher from myself.

      If, after both of those considerations have been met, I am selling a product that undersells others or hurts the market through glut, I will not give a fig. I have met my ethical obligations, as I see them, and will let the market fend for itself.

      If I cannot publish while meeting both of those considerations, I will not publish. Because those things, the ethics and the quality, are more important to me than money or status. If that helps or hurts the market, I will not give a fig.

      Folks that can find a way to make it work with both of those considerations, even if it is where I could not, will get all my respect, and probably my business. (If they can get my attention and time as discussed above.) Those that publish without doing either will not.

      LPJ, Mongoose, and similar outfits do not get my business. They could be big, they could be small. They could have a business plan, they could not. I do not care. They treat their people badly, they produce frequently poor material, and so they've lost my business and my respect.

      OTOH, Mob United or Matt Wilson's stuff I'll pick up because I know, specifically and with detail, that they treat their people well and that they are committed to producing quality. They could be big, they could be small, they could have a business plan, they could not. I don't give a fuck. They treat people well, they produce good stuff, and so yay for them.

      I'm interested in quality products and treating people well. I have no interest in the market except in as much as it makes, or stops, that from happening.

      Posted By: chaduThat is exactly the point I've been trying to make.
      \nHuh. Okay, Chad, if that's your point, then I'm not sure what we can do to help, especially if you're mostly talking about the PDF market. If people are making games that are considered comparable (in value) to your games, but are selling them for less because they aren't trying to make money... I can definitely see how that might cut into your sales. But I guess I'm not sure what you can do about it, other than talking to them directly.

      On a related note, I have heard small press print publishers talking about pricing in ways similar to what you're talking about. Saying things like, "Well, if your book costs X amount for each copy, you should clearly sell your book for X+Y dollars, because doing otherwise is devaluing all of indie rpgs for everyone" (pissing in the pool basically). I have a hard time knowing how to respond to that, honestly, because I don't publish Push (and people don't contribute to Push) in order to make money (though it does make money for both me and the other contributors) and it's priced at a point that we don't make that much off of each sale. But, because other people have different business plans in mind (ones that involve a certain amount of income per book and, therefore, a certain sticker price), I should raise my prices?

      That just seems against the spirit of indie publishing, honestly. The whole reason I'm publishing it myself is so I can make all the important decisions about the product and conduct business however I want to conduct business. Certainly, I will consider how my actions affect my fellow publishers, but that doesn't mean I will necessarily participate in pushing up the "standard sticker price" for an indie product, just so other publishers will feel better about the decision to charge X+Y for their products or get more sales because their products won't seem "overpriced" (one trait that is determined by consensus, I suppose).

      I'm sorry that your sales are going down, perhaps due to hobbyist publishers, perhaps due to folks who are taking advantage of their contributors to keep prices low. Those seem like two different problems, however. But, as a hobbyist publisher with very different priorities, I'm not sure what I can do to help you (or folks in the small press print scene that feel similarly) without destroying my own "business plan" and enjoyment as a hobbyist publisher. I do this because I love doing it and to do otherwise would mean losing a lot of that love.

      \n I'm seeing a handful of issues here, all getting conflated.

      Capitalism
      People set prices according to supply and demand and what they think the labor and final goods are worth. It's Econ 101 stuff. It's all fair, given that everyone has the same opportunities.

      In the case of game publishing, we all have the same opportunities -- indie and industry. Whether we use those profits for rent or for beer, it's money in our pocket.

      Hobby or Profession?
      I can't tell the difference. Chad works a day job and then does RPG freelance stuff. He says he needs that money to pay bills, and this makes him a "professional game designer." [Edit: Chad said he needs money to pay bills, but didn't say that it made him a pro. The quotes were not his. Sorry for any confusion.] If his day job paid more (covered all his family's needs), would he consider himself a hobbyist game designer? If Vincent makes more money selling his games than Chad makes selling his, is Vincent a professional, too?

      In the end, I find the distinction pretty meaningless. If you do it for money, you're a professional. If you do it for free, you're a hobbyist.

      Fairness
      If you're a publisher that is exploiting people, I can see that it's a problem and I agree you're not competing fairly. People can sell their labor for whatever they like.

      I don't pay myself for the writing (and rewriting) I did on Verge. When I publish it, I hope to break even; better, I hope to make a little profit so I can afford to publish my next project. I want to pay artists and editors what they're worth but, the fact is, I can't afford to. I found some new artists and offered them advertising space in the back of my game instead of money. I now have a moral obligation to get that game published so I can repay them for their time and talent. I am basically begging friends to do editing and layout for me, or I'll have to do it myself.

      Does this mean I'm not fairly competing with other publishers who pay artists and editors market rate? I don't think so. I spend way more time than they do trying to find people who can do this stuff for me on the cheap, or for free. My labor is my cost, and I pay myself nothing.

      Is paying myself nothing "fair"? Sure. My labor is worth what I feel it's worth. I am doing what I love. I am sure that Chad loves what he does, too. He just takes it more seriously than I do, so he can eat.

      Will my products take money out of Chad's hands? Maybe. I hope not, but it's a reality I have to face. Is it unfair if it does? Why should it be? If I am willing to spend my time to write and publish my game for less than he will do it, then that's the value of my game to me. If I sell it for less than he sells a comparable product, then that's the way the market falls. I haven't exploited anyone to get here, except maybe myself, and I do it for love.
      \n

      Chad, this has been my disconnect with Malcolm's earlier posts about unfair business practices. He has posted multiple times that the "standard practice" involves contributors being screwed, but when I think about the indie games people that form the bulk of game publishers on Story Games (the people I see at GenCon, at various booths and stopping by Games on Demand), I don't think they pursue those kinds of "standard practices." But I really don't know how common they are in other roleplaying publishing communities. For example, I have no clue about what standard practices are like on RPGnow or DriveThru. The PDF publishing community is, in many ways, its own thing and many of the small press print publishers involved with IPR aren't closely involved with that community. There are some exceptions, however. But, overall, from the information that I have seen, I don't think that IPR's publishers underpay their contributors.
      \nWell, Jonathan, there's an obvious disconnect between your claim that this doesn't happen in the community and the fact that people *in* this community, *in* this thread are arguing that they should be able to do that which apparently never happens.
      \n
      Adam DrayCapitalism
      People set prices according to supply and demand and what they think the labor and final goods are worth. It's Econ 101 stuff. It's all fair, given that everyone has the same opportunities.

      In the case of game publishing, we all have the same opportunities -- indie and industry. Whether we use those profits for rent or for beer, it's money in our pocket
      \nAside from the fact that the pocket money thing has evidently not been the case (publishers who couldn't afford Gen Con unless they made sales *at* Gen Con) this reminds me of the time one of my profs asked this question:

      "If you could press a button that made everyone equally poor or one that made everyone equally wealthy, which one would you press?"

      You had some wags talk about how it would all be the same thing. Then the prof replied.

      "What the hell is wrong with you? Poverty and wealth actually mean something aside from comparing yourself to your neighbor."

      There aren't really "equal opportunities" unless they're lousy. It's a good idea to make the worst opportunities better.

      Posted By: Matt WilsonPosted By: Adam DrayI'm seeing a handful of issues here, all getting conflated.
      \nEverything Adam said, yeah, me too. Although I do think of myself as being paid for the time I spent writingPrimetime Adventures. It's just coming in installments.

      As the publisher, you're assuming the risk for the chance of an open-ended reward. Nothing wrong with that.

      \n

      \n
      \n
      \n But who decides that art is worth $100 per page of b&w? Stuff is worth what people will pay.

      Saying that paying less is "screwing somebody over" assumes that they feel exploited. If they're happy with the arrangement, it's not exploitation. Maybe they like the project and want to help out. Maybe they don't think their stuff is worth $100 per page.

      If they think the price I am offering is exploitive, they don't have to take the deal. It's a free market. If their stuff is worth $100/page, they'll find a buyer at that rate.

      Maybe I'm missing something vital. I'm not entrenched in this position. I'd like to understand why people think a negotiated price below (even far below) market average is exploitive.

      You had some wags talk about how it would all be the same thing. Then the prof replied.

      Posted By: Adam DrayIf I sell my game for $2, and you buy it, are you exploiting me? Are you immoral?
      \nI can buy any number of strawmen for any price.

      If you publish a game for two bucks, you're taking the risk in exchange for an open-ended reward. When you hire someone for next to nothing, they are neither being rewarded fairly for their labour nor offered the same chance of a benefit longer down the road. That's why you either offer payment keyed to actual sales or a flat rate that pays someone well for their labour.

      I say the same sorts of things when people defend beating their spouses. Really, I'm incorrigible when people wax poetic about their right to abuse other people.

      Posted By: Malcolm Sheppard
      I can buy any number of strawmen for any price.

      If you publish a game for two bucks, you're taking the risk in exchange for an open-ended reward. When you hire someone for next to nothing, they are neither being rewarded fairly for their labour nor offered the same chance of a benefit longer down the road. That's why you either offer payment keyed to actual sales or a flat rate that pays someone well for their labour.
      \nI don't see it as a strawman. I see it as a pretty direct analogy.

      If I publish a game for two bucks, I have chosen to sell my hard work for a certain price. I have a number of transactions like this with multiple customers.
      If I sell an hour of my drawing time for two bucks, I have done the same thing. I can make a number of similar transactions with multiple customers.

      Posted By: Adam DrayPosted By: Malcolm Sheppard
      I say the same sorts of things when people defend beating their spouses. Really, I'm incorrigible when people wax poetic about their right to abuse other people.
      \nThis is an absurd and fallacious analogy. Abused spouses are not consenting partners. Artists who agree to work for less than $100/page are consenting. They can say no to the price.

      If I offer Jennifer half of what she usually charges for a cover -- or if I ask her to do it for me for free, even -- then she will say no.       Consent is what happens when people are able to make a choice without pressure from surrounding social arrangements. A community that devalues an artist work is just that pressure. By making your argument you are in fact continuing that pressure, contributing to it and reducing opportunities for real consent.

      Posted By: Malcolm SheppardPeople who have invested time and effort in their profession can't just drop it and do something else -- it they could, they wouldn't show up for the "interview."
      \nAlright, look: I'm a big leftie. I actively support unions, I'm a big proponent of raising the minimum wage, I strongly support affirmative action, etc. But what we're talking about, here, are people who decide not to go work for an employet, but to be freelancers. That's a whole different economic decision. It's risky. People take that road because of the advantages it offers: be your own boss, pick your own projects, do what you love. That's awesome! And I wish them much success. But you can't expect freelancers and self-employed contractors to be granted the same kind of protections as regular employees.

      If we're actually talking about salaried paralegals, you know what, I whole-heartedly agree with you. They should at the least be paid minimum wage. Maybe they should even unionize.

      Fuck, maybe artists should unionize. Then they can counter the social environment that keeps the rates low.

      But absent that, exactly because of self-employment and the freedom you're gaining from it, you need to take the risks that come with it. As someone who's looked into self-employment quite a bit during the last year, that's just the way it looks to me.

      ETA: In case this didn't come through, freelancers should ALSO be paid at least minimum wage. And the minimum wage should be high enough to allow for subsistence, and sadly I don't think we're at that point--it should really be higher.
      \n

      Posted By: Malcolm SheppardThat's because the answer to your question does not reside with a person -- it informs a person. I'm sorry if your desire to corral the conversation with a leading question was stymied.
      \nIt's a fairly straightforward question, Malcolm. Since you apparently lack any sort of real-world experience with the subject, I'll offer some to you.

      As an employer, I am competing for talent. If I offer some ridiculous shit wage, I can't get employees who can fulfill my needs because they're in short supply at that pay rate. So I keep upping my offered rate until I break that threshold: I get the talent I need at the lowest rate the employee will tolerate. I trust my employees to know what it costs to keep a roof over their own heads. It cannot be my responsibility to second-guess their needs, because there's an inherent conflict of interest in that. It would be, in a phrase you so gleefully toss around, immoral for me to interfere with the success of my company (which includes me and many other employees) by doing so.

      When it comes to artists and writers in the RPG business -- and, yes, Malcolm, you and I have worked for some of the same clients -- you find absurdly low rates of pay for all levels of work. When I finally left the business, I had topped out at about $0.35/word. My very next job, freelancing at a magazine, started me at $1/word and went up from there. $250 for painted cover art was not unusual; $10-20 per quarter-page filler art as well.

      Rates in the RPG business are low because the supply greatly outstrips the demand. It's the simplest math in the world. Employers in the RPG business are faced with exactly the same choices that I am: how do you get the talent you need at the lowest possible rate? You start as low as you can. If you get satisfactory work at that rate, then you've found your rate. If it's unsatisfactory for any reason (substandard quality, missed deadlines, pissy attitude), pay more until you find the talent.

      The problem, Malcolm, isn't the publishers. It's the consumers. As long as consumers of RPG products continue to tolerate the current level of writing, editing, artwork, production, design, etc, there's no motive for anyone anywhere to change anything. Consumers have proven time and time again that they're willing to pay for art that only cost the publisher $20. They continue to gleefully spend money for supplements that cost the publisher a dime a word to write.

      It's always sad to see political ideology bump up against the real world, Malcolm, but your whole premise is beyond absurd. It's infantile.

      p.

      \n I'm wondering:

      Let's say that right now we have people working as artists that can get enough work for 1 B&W page per day. That takes them an hour but fine - we should pay them $100 because that's a good day's work. Everybody who can't afford this gets out of the business of buying art.

      Five years later, a glut of new artists have gone through school. Now artists are only able to get enough work for 1 B&W page per week.

      It's hard for them to retrain, and we have a duty to pay a living wage. Do we have a moral obligation to pay them $500 for 1 black and white page?
